Sam Altman on Building the Future of AI

OpenAI(1.9M) ·46:11 ·23.8K views · 2026-04-07
Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI) Josh Achiam (OpenAI) Adrien Ecoffet (OpenAI)
OpenAI leadership warns that the transition to superintelligence is imminent, necessitating an immediate "New Industrial Policy" to build massive compute infrastructure, reform tax systems, and create AI-driven "defensive shields" against cyber and biological threats.
TL;DR OpenAI leadership warns that the transition to superintelligence is imminent, necessitating an immediate "New Industrial Policy" to build massive compute infrastructure, reform tax systems, and create AI-driven "defensive shields" against cyber and biological threats. The...

TL;DR

OpenAI leadership warns that the transition to superintelligence is imminent, necessitating an immediate "New Industrial Policy" to build massive compute infrastructure, reform tax systems, and create AI-driven "defensive shields" against cyber and biological threats.

Accelerated Timelines and the "COVID Moment"

  • The Pace of Progress: Sam Altman emphasizes that the rate of AI advancement is accelerating, moving toward powerful models that will impact the global economy within a few years.
  • The "Pre-Symptomatic" Warning: Altman compares the current moment to January 2020, where researchers see a massive shift coming that society has not yet "digested."
  • The 2028 Milestone: Adrien Ecoffet notes OpenAI’s official goal is to develop an automated AI researcher by March 2028, a breakthrough that would likely create a "double whammy" of disruption by exponentially accelerating further AI progress.

Societal Resilience and Emergent Defense

  • Moving Beyond "Classical" Safety: The panel argues that safety cannot rely solely on a few companies "aligning" their models. As open-source models proliferate, society must develop an emergent, decentralized response.
  • Cybersecurity Defenses: AI will inevitably be used to find software vulnerabilities. Resilience requires giving trusted defenders "differential access" to frontier models to patch systems before they can be exploited.
  • Biological "Shields": To mitigate the risk of AI-assisted pathogen development, the world needs a "defense shield" consisting of advanced detection systems and rapid-response treatments.
  • Institutional Innovation: Joshua Achiam suggests creating "in-between" institutions (bridging the gap between private corporations and slow-moving government) to prototype safety nets and monitor economic shifts in real-time.

Democratization Through Infrastructure

  • Compute as a Human Right: OpenAI advocates for a "Universal Basic Compute" model, ensuring individuals and small startups have access to the processing power needed to innovate.
  • Preventing Power Concentration: Altman argues that unless compute infrastructure is made abundant and cheap (similar to the history of electricity), the technology will be monopolized by the wealthiest entities bidding up the price of scarce resources.
  • The Future of Startups: AI significantly lowers the friction of entrepreneurship. The panel envisions a future where "two or three people and a ton of GPUs" can build a company that previously required hundreds of employees.

Reimagining the Economy and Labor

  • Tax and Benefit Reform: As AI performs more intellectual work, the panel suggests shifting the tax base away from human labor and toward AI-generated output.
  • Worker Empowerment:
      • Portable Benefits: Decoupling healthcare and other benefits from specific employers to support a more fluid, AI-driven workforce.
      • Countercyclical Measures: Exploring ideas like a 32-hour work week and enhanced unemployment insurance as tools to manage disruption.
      • Union Involvement: Empowering workers and unions to co-author how AI is deployed in the workplace to prevent invasive surveillance.
  • The Shift to Agency: Joshua Achiam notes that the economic value will shift toward human "agency"—the ability to use AI tools to handle complex functions (legal, coding, marketing) that previously required specialized teams.

The Persistence of Human Value

  • Timeless Qualities: Despite AI’s reasoning capabilities, human qualities like character, compassion, effort, and creativity will become more valuable.
  • The "Care Economy": Human-to-human connection remains a premium. Altman shares that "robot cafes" highlight a deep human desire for social interaction (the "barista's smile") that technology cannot fulfill.
  • Personalized Empowerment: AI will act as a high-level thought partner, scanning personal data to suggest ideas and helping individuals overcome "schlep blindness"—the resignation to old, inefficient ways of living.

***

"The more time the public, our leaders, and the political system has to debate ideas before you really have to make a decision, the more likely you are to make a good decision. We expect to be in a world of extremely capable models quite soon." — Sam Altman
"I think what AI and superintelligence will unlock is the freedom to [provide food, shelter, and healthcare] at a much lower cost than has ever been possible... it benefits everyone and not just the very wealthy." — Joshua Achiam
"Once you have this automated researcher... capable of doing AI research, you potentially have a double whammy of disruption. It might accelerate further AI progress... more than the pace we've been making so far." — Adrien Ecoffet
Good afternoon everyone and welcome to the OpenAI Forum. I'm Chris Nicholson and I'm glad to be here with all of you today. The forum is a place for serious conversation about how AI is being used in the world, what we're learning from that, and how more people can help shape its trajectory. Today's conversation focuses on one of the biggest questions in technology: what it will mean as AI systems grow dramatically more capable and how we should think about their implications for science, work, our life together, and governance. To discuss that, I'm joined by Sam Altman, Co-Founder and CEO of OpenAI, Joshua Achiam, OpenAI's Chief Futurist, and Adrien Ecoffet, a longtime researcher here. So let's get started. Sam, the blueprint we released this morning talks a lot about superintelligence. A big question on my mind is: why are we doing that now and what are some things that you can see from the inside that you wish everybody knew? The the biggest reason is simply that the rate of progress is continuing to accelerate and we believe we are very close now. This won't be a one-time thing; this will be, you know, over the next few years, a move to powerful models that will impact the world in important ways. The researchers that are working on these models did, I think, an incredible job with this set of ideas. These are meant to be early ideas to start a discussion. You know, I'm sure we'll get to much better ideas as the world debates all of these. They are staring down the models that are coming, the pipeline in front of us. And you know, we may be wrong, we may hit some wall, we are imperfect. But given what we see, we expect to be in a world of extremely capable models quite soon and then for the the ramp of the capability to continue to increase. I think this will have huge impacts on the economy, on the way we live, on what we can do. One thing I've observed from watching the world go through some number of transitions is that the more time the the public, our leaders, the political system has to debate ideas before you really have to make a decision, the more likely you are to make a good decision. So starting this now, given what we see coming, so that as this becomes a very large issue of public debate, I think is important. For sure. And speaking of debate, we brought in a lot of researchers, Adrien, very early in this process. I think it was a maybe a unique exercise here in how many researchers were working with the folks who also think about policy a lot. It was closely tied. What was that like for you and for the research group as a whole? Yeah, you know I think it was, well, it was a very interesting experience. It was my first time working, like, actively on a policy doc. I think it was a little bit like humbling in some cases. You know, sometimes as researchers we can have these abstract ideas of, "Oh, we should really be thinking about the economic impact of this," or about, like, policy for for safety. But you know, it's one thing to think about this and another thing to, like, actually put, you know, pen to paper and and think of concrete policy ideas that are going to be debated by your peer researchers. So that was an interesting experience for me. I I hope it was, you know, very helpful for the final product that we had the researchers involved so early and so and so deeply. Like, for something like this especially since it's so forward looking, you kind of need to Sam's point—like you need people who are who are dealing with this technology every day, who know how to build it, know how the safety stack works, and also are seeing, like, the speed of progress. One thing that, you know, that I remember as part of this process in the in the past few months that we were we were working on it, like a lot of researchers went through a a transition from, you know, writing most of their own code to having AI mostly write most of their code. And I think that, I think to some extent, led them to bring a lot of the urgency of, like, this technology is is real and important and moving fast in a way that maybe not everyone can can see. And that's part of also kind of your your earlier question about why now, like, because we are seeing this urgency. Can I tell a quick story that reminds me of? There was a night in, I guess it would have been late January, early February of 2020, when the OpenAI researchers kind of got obsessed with COVID before the rest of the world did. And we were talking about it all the time and we're watching numbers every day and we're like, "this is going to happen." And you know, we were like making plans to go work from home and there was, like, some article that came out, like, mocking us because they're like, "these crazy people at OpenAI." We had put, like, some copper or something on some of the door handles because people are... remember? You remember that? You remember that? And some journalist wrote about this and there's all all these things going on. And I and and you know, we kind of like made our plans and we assumed a shutdown was going to happen and we're like, "there's this thing coming, the world's not paying attention." And for whatever reason, something about, like, working on exponential makes you understand these things better. So I think we were like a group of people that were primed to do it. And then there was this one night, it was, like, a very cold night. I lived in the Mission at the time, and I was like, "I'm about to get locked in my house for a while and I'm going to go for a walk. I want one more time I'm going to, you know, go for a walk 'cause who knows what's going to happen." And I went through this long walk through the city, hours, cold, cold night and I was like watching people, you know, breathing in each other's faces in restaurants and bars through the windows. And I was like wearing my mask or, you know, looking crazy. And there was one other dude out wearing a mask and kind of we nodded at each other and that was it, but other than that it was just like life felt totally normal. I have not felt that so acutely as I do again in this moment, where it's like there is this crazy change. The change has already happened, like the models have already hit some level. Society has not digested them yet. We feel like we see it clearly, we are trying to tell the world that it's gonna happen. It is hard to get this across, but it feels like that night at the very beginning of COVID walking through the streets again. For sure. And speaking of debate, we brought in a lot of researchers, Adrien, very early in this process. I think it was a maybe a unique exercise here in how many researchers were working with the folks who also think about policy a lot. It was closely tied. What was that like for you and for the research group as a whole? I will answer that. I think the positives are so positive we should talk more about the all of things that we're thinking about here but like you mentioned science. If we can really go make a decades' worth of scientific progress in a year, if we can go cure a ton of diseases, if we can come up with personalized medicine for people, find new materials to sort of make cheap safe energy... There's—if we can make it such that anybody who can come up with an idea for a startup can have the AI implement it, write the piece of software they want, you know, have a custom video game that's, like, the most fun for you to play... this stuff is all wonderful. Now, you know, part of the reason for putting this out with urgency is there are going to come, there are a lot of things coming that we'll need to mitigate. I assume we'll figure it out. I I'm always an optimistic person by nature. But also, the benefits of this technology so change the option space in front of us and what we can do that one of my first reactions to reading the blueprint after the researchers wrote it was, like, "It is awesome but also a little crazy that we can credibly be talking about these kinds of things." So we do have, like, if the AI is as good as we think and all these wonderful things happen, we also have an incredible new tool at our disposal to help us mitigate the potential downsides. Yeah. And it's a tool for everyone. Joshua, I'd like to ask you... so I think we've talked about this as social infrastructure that changes the way people work and learn and participate in society. What what responsibilities do we have, and do institutions have, to help society get ready for that, and how do you think about it? Yeah. You know, one of the things that I think about here, this does kind of come back to the broad benefits for everyone thing. For a long time in society, we've had this great aspiration that everyone could have, you know, food, shelter, electricity, healthcare. And we've we've always wanted to see there be some kind of new step in the direction of providing these sorts of things for everyone so that everyone can focus on the things that are most important in their lives. And we've often been told over and over again, like, "You know, actually we can't have that, it's too expensive, there's no way to pay for it," "it's like it's nice but it would be too burdensome to society to do all of that." And I think what AI and superintelligence will unlock is the freedom to do all of it at a much lower cost than has ever been possible. And correspondingly, for the folks of us who are sort of the stewards of the technology, we have a special responsibility to make sure that we actually do fully realize those benefits and be a part of building policy and systems that help working people, middle class people, people in low income countries really make sure that it benefits everyone and not just the very wealthy. So I'm I those are the things that I think of as key responsibilities here and I'm very excited about it. Plus, the downside risks are also quite serious and there are systemic shocks that could happen. We have to prepare, we have to be thoughtful, we have to be honest about them. Yeah. Yeah. So that sounds like a resilience question and and when I have looked at the document and and speak with you folks, it sounds like you think of resilience in layers, right? There's kind of—and and a lot of it is not before the AI goes out but it's also after the AI goes out in terms of our responses to AI and how people are are prepared for it. I'd actually like to ask each of you how you think about resilience. Adrien, why don't why don't we start with you. Yeah, you know, I I think maybe one distinction that I would draw with you know classically we've thought about about safety and about making sure that we that we run you know safety evals, that we red team our models, that we implement mitigations. And I think, like, that's, you know, to your point of resilience is in layer, this is, like, a very important layer that we should, you know, still keep doing and keep expanding. But at the same time, you want society to be prepared for the possibility that, well, maybe there will be some actors who do less safety testing and like what what happens what happens then? How is society resilient to risks from AIs in these cases? Maybe there will be incidents in spite of our safety testing or near misses. In the in the blueprint we talk, for instance, about incident reporting that's modeled a little bit of after how the aviation industry does things when whenever there's kind of like a near miss or any incident—like however minor—that kind of like gets reported to a to a database so that you know all the companies can kind of know, "Okay, well this is a risk and perhaps you know here are mitigations that that you could implement." And so there's a lot of things that could happen at a society-wide level. Another thing would be, you know, defending against against risks. We're talking about models that that can code a lot better now. That also implies cyber capabilities, like could they help bad actors run cyber attacks? And I think part of resilience is ensuring that we that we make our software systems more secure and use AI to do these things. So so again, there is as you said all these layers, so that's kind of how I take the the resilience question. Sam, before the show we were talking about how prosperity can be emergent when everybody has access to AI. It also strikes me that resilience can be emergent. How how do you think about it? Yeah, I I think our original AI safety thinking and the fields' in general—I'll call it classical AI safety thinking—was that there going to be a very tiny number of AIs in the world, the only thing that matters is making sure those AIs do the right thing and that as long as you align them and they don't do unsafe things, the world will be okay. I think the picture now is actually more stable but more complex and there are going to be many AIs in the world. And the it will not be enough to just say, like, you know, "this one company is going to make sure the AI never does something it shouldn't do," but there will need to be an emergent response across society. You know, Adrien talked a little bit about some of this, but if we just take a few examples of threats that we expect to be coming, cyber security is definitely going to become a huge issue. AI will be incredibly good at finding vulnerabilities in software and I think the world will find that their software is much more brittle and much less secure than we thought. And you know, humans just have limited capacity to find the exploits. It is not enough to say that, you know, one or two or three model providers are just going to make sure that their systems won't do this, because code is like you know the same thing that code is very useful to be good at and being good at writing code can also help find security problems. And even if all of us somehow could prevent our models from ever being used for this, there will be open source models coming soon that are that are good at code and thus good at security exploits. So what has to happen is the world has to use these models and there can be, you know, differential access. You can give it to good known trusted defenders first—we have a program for that, other companies will do similar things. And you have to empower the companies that defend software because there will be some power plant that no one has understood the software for 20 years and no one can patch it and there's a big problem with it and you have to do something about that. But a resilience approach here is: "Okay there's this new thing in the world, there's AI that is really good at exploiting computer systems, let's use AI to defend it." And that is not a one company thing; that's going to require this huge effort. If we go a little bit further, I think there will be a bio version of this where you know classically people have said, "Well, we're just going to restrict our models from being able to develop pathogens." Someone at some point is going to use some model to develop a pathogen and the world needs defense shields against that. Detection systems, rapid response treatment, a whole bunch of other things. And this is not—this doesn't get us off the hook in any way of aligning our systems and building safe systems. We still have to do that; like, we get a time advantage there if as long as we stay at the frontier. But we do need the world to, like, do its thing, society to have this emergent magic and build these layers of defensive shields. There are many threats besides those two, but that's enough given we have short time. For sure. Joshua, you have shared some interesting thoughts with me about how each huge technological shift has produced new institutions and new democratic mechanisms, and also you've been thinking a lot about new institutions that might emerge now. How are you—what are your what are your most exciting ideas and the what do you really like to think about in terms of the collective response to superintelligence? Yeah, certainly. So so one thing I'll say first on the subject of resilience, just as grounding: a lot of the problems that we're concerned about AI creating new externalities for are problems and vulnerabilities that exist in the world regardless of whether AI is present, and AI just increases the urgency of action. But coming back to COVID, we found back then that everyone had a much deeper dependency on the functioning of supply chains than most people were previously conscious of. Supply chains are super important: supply chains for food, for goods, for for everything that sustains civilization. And also of course, you know, for other types of vulnerabilities like democracy, we've been debating for years how there are real risks when people are influencing society sort of inappropriately. And we're we're worried that AI is going to make these things potentially easier to attack in the near term because there will be tools at people's disposal that they haven't had before. I'm excited about the possibility that we can build new institutions and state capacities to use AI to rectify some of these vulnerabilities. We can systematically identify them with AI in ways we couldn't in the past, we can systematically close them in ways we couldn't in the past, and we can use AI to scale up efforts to combat certain types of issues in in ways where we can potentially make it too expensive for attackers to really do something. So you know, on cyber and bio, I am optimistic that maybe we can build an ecosystem of defenders that all together can make it so expensive for attackers to try to, you know, run a cyber attack that there just won't be that much of an incentive to do it. And if we can fully implement the the bio resilience side of things not just for pathogens that might impact humans but especially for the food supply chain... this is one where I have, like, a hobby horse on this and I'm going to talk about it every chance I get. I think people underattend to food supply chain bio risks, but we can use AI to make that resilient at scale in a way that today is cost prohibitive. So I'm very excited about, you know, the things we can build there. Yeah, neat. One more question, when we talk about kind of the individual transition for many people—what work looks like, where value shifts to—I know you've been thinking about that a lot. How how—what do you see happening as folks kind of transition to other ways of creating value in an AI economy? Oh oh goodness, that's very broad. I think people will have a lot more opportunity to exercise agency. You know, if you can start a new business and have a team of AIs that handle all of the functions of putting the business together that you have no expertise in yourself, you can get something off the ground an awful lot easier. And so, you know, there are a lot of ways that the economy just fundamentally changes when you give people access and tools to help them do more things than they could have before. Yeah, for sure. Sam, similar question. You have so much experience with startups: running them, managing Y Combinator, or just being in the ecosystem for so long. What—how do you see startups changing and like our potential to realize new ideas changing with AI? I'm obsessed with trying to explore the space. I don't know exactly what it's going to look like but this idea of one person or a very small team being able to create an entire startup, as Joshua was talking about, pretty quickly... all my instincts are there's something deep and important to figure out here. Every time in our industry that the friction, cost, whatever you want to call it, of starting a startup has come down a lot, amazing new things have happened. You know, I remember the transition—the one of these transitions that I was doing a startup during—was when like basically AWS came out and all of a sudden there was like an idea of a cloud and a small startup didn't have to go do all the crazy things you used to have to do, manage its racks in the closet. And that was like an amazing change to what you could do as a small startup with a few people. This one that's coming is much bigger and there have been several in between, but I really want to find out what it looks like when a startup is, you know, two or three people and a ton of GPUs and you can just you can really democratize who can start a startup. Yeah, yeah, yeah, it's that democratization aspect and it gets down to the widespread availability of AI. How do you—what's the best frame for thinking about bringing more people in, democratizing AI access? I think when people talk about democratization of AI, they they mean two different things. One is shared access and making sure that everybody gets to use sufficient AI to improve their own lives, build things for other people, all that. And the other is a sort of voice in where it's all going to go. I think both are very important. Part of why we do things like this blueprint are if we're not debating the issues as a society... also part of the reason we actually release products in the first place. People don't have a feel for this if people aren't talking about this; that is kind of a prerequisite to people being able to have input. But it's not enough; you also have to have a way that people's input, that we listen and the input is captured back into into the system. So that's one that I think is really important. And the other is we need to put not just services like ChatGPT but the real deal, high compute, valuable services where people can start a startup or make a scientific discovery or whatever, in broadly into many people's hands. Yeah. Yeah. And that's going to take new economic models or much cheaper inference to to get it more widespread, right? And that's an infrastructure problem among many other problems to solve. You know, we we used to talk for years in OpenAI about when we were going to get through the compute crunch, when we were going to build, like, enough compute that we wouldn't be so strapped. I don't think we ever get out of it. If we do our job, if we keep driving the cost of intelligence down and the capabilities of intelligence up, I think effectively if that happens, the demand is uncapped. And the worlds where we don't build enough infrastructure, I think you get a crazy concentration of power and a concentration of compute because people will just bid the price up and up and up. So the only thing that I really believe in as a long-term democratization strategy is to just make so much AI infrastructure available and make the models so good and so capable that we hope to get to a world where people are like, "I need help coming up with ideas for it to use all this compute for." I don't think we will in practice. But I certainly think that if compute is very limited, you know, the richest people and the richest companies in the world will just sort of bid up the price to a kind of extreme degree. It'll be another kind of scarcity that that is monopolized. Where—so this is—more data centers is actually a very egalitarian initiative in the sense that they can make AI access more widespread. I I would look at many examples throughout history. You know, one of the best things that we ever did for really increasing people's quality of life was to drive the price of electricity way down. Price of energy broadly way down. Energy correlates incredibly well with quality of life, or at least it has for a long time. Maybe now it will be more about AI. And by making energy abundant and shockingly cheap relative to what it cost 50, 100, 200 years ago, that has done, like, quite a bit for lifting the entire world up. I think we need to do the same thing with AI and I think that means you need a lot of it and just like with energy, you need to innovate new ways to make it much more affordable. Can I give give a thought on on this whole issue as well? I think we've talked a bit about, you know, the broad access to AI which I think is very important and and can create, like, potentially a lot of new products that will be very helpful for the world, very useful for people. I think one thing that, you know, we try to think about as well in in writing this blueprint is, you know, ensuring that you know ordinary people who maybe, you know, aren't going to start a startup necessarily, all of them, like aren't left behind by this technology. I think this is related to to something that we might talk about which is kind of: how does AI change the composition of the economy? Does it move it more towards capital or towards, like, labor, but labor that that is done by by AI really? And one of the things that we talk about in the blueprint is how do you modernize the the tax base for an economy that is like this? And how do you distribute kind of this prosperity that, you know, will be created by this technology? How to make sure that this is prosperity for everyone and not like wealth for, you know, a relatively few few people? And so this is something that we tried to address in the blueprint, but I'm curious if we can talk about it here as well. We we totally should. A couple of the big ideas that I saw were kind of workers co-authoring how AI is deployed in their workplaces. I'd like to ask you about that Josh and I'd also ask like to ask all you about more broadly how society can capture the upside. What are the institutional forms that that'll take? But let's start with you Joshua. Sure, just on this compute question for one second I want to say: the compute allocation problem, figuring out what things we use compute to help people do with AI, will probably be one of the most important society-wide questions to navigate over the next few years while compute is relatively scarce. And we should try to boost the amount of compute in the world as much and as quickly as we possibly can so we don't have to have painful tradeoff questions where there's some extraordinary good we could provide to everyone and we're stuck with a hard question where someone says, "Well, how are you going to pay for that?" because the cost of compute is so high. On getting workers involved in AI, I I actually I kind of want to back up and just acknowledge an elephant in the room, which is that a lot of workers are concerned about AI. They're worried about what AI means for them. They are not immediately excited at the prospect of of figuring out, "All right, how are we going to use AI in our workplace?" They're thinking, "Oh my gosh, is the AI going to replace me?" and what I what I think is sort of the important first step here is that those of us who are working on AI and who are kind of the stewards for this, have to be putting out things like this this blueprint document where we say, "well here's how we're going to advocate for policies to make sure that the economy is fair and that you are supported no matter what." And then given that we've made this level of safety net, now we can talk about, you know, something where where you have confidence in in us. We can talk together, have a good conversation. We should figure out how do we empower unions to make wise choices about where and how to use AI; how do we empower workers to participate in conversations about the acceptable use of AI in the workplace. I think a lot of folks are rightly concerned about AI surveillance in the workplace. Making sure that workers are a part of the decisions about those types of things feels very important. And a big push on AI literacy to ensure that folks, you know, get the tools they need to use AI to make their lives better, to start small businesses, to to do all the kinds of things that, you know, will help them realize the potential. For sure. What are some institutions that you've considered that allow everyone to capture some of the upside, Josh? So let's see. For for new kinds of institutions, I think state capacity to measure pieces of the economy in, you know, greater granular depth so that there can be responses if there's, you know, like an economic shift in one place or another, to monitor, yeah. I think AI is actually a very exciting tool for making that more scalable and less expensive than it would have been in the past. I also think that there can be more institutions that are kind of in between corporations and governments which have very, very different levels of accountability governance-wise. There maybe is like a need for something with, like, more in-between level of accountability that can provide, you know, like social safety net type services or something between a corporate board and the regulator? Yeah, yeah, yeah, like this—this can't all just happen in private companies that have very minimal governance, and we also don't expect that government, which moves fairly slowly, is going to do all of it quite quickly. And so we sort of maybe need some in-between institutions that can help us prototype things. This is, like, a little bit spitballing, and I don't think this is, like, quite covered in the blueprint but you know, you asked, and so that's—I think in the in the vein of new institutions—the kind of thing we could do. Cool. Sam, institutionally how are you envisioning that people might broadly participate in the upside? I think we talked earlier about very broad access and giving a lot of people a lot of compute. I you you hear these ideas like universal basic compute or other things with nice branding, but really what they mean is just like, instead of the traditional thinking of "we're going to give people, you know, a monthly stipend or money or whatever when AI does all the jobs," I think it's way better to say: actually people are pretty good about knowing what they need and pretty creative about figuring out how to use things. But if people are boxed out of access to this resource, that will be a challenge. I do suspect that we're going to have to make changes to how we tax. Like in a world where AI is doing most of the intellectual work in the world or at least of the work of today, you know, we probably are going to need to explore some way to tax that instead of taxing human income in the traditional sense. I suspect that we will need to provide new kinds of transition assistance, unemployment insurance, things like that. And I suspect that eventually we will need to think about how people get to be an owner in the upside of all of this in new ways. AI Won't Take Your Job—It Will Make You the CEO | The a16z Show

a16z(262.0K) ·65:45 ·7.9K views · 2026-04-07
Erik Torenberg (General Partner, a16z) Balaji Srinivasan (Angel Investor and Entrepreneur)
Artificial intelligence is shifting the economic landscape by drastically reducing the cost of creation while simultaneously increasing the cost of verification, effectively turning every individual into a "CEO" who must manage, prompt, and audit a fleet of AI agents.
TL;DR Artificial intelligence is shifting the economic landscape by drastically reducing the cost of creation while simultaneously increasing the cost of verification, effectively turning every individual into a "CEO" who must manage, prompt, and audit a fleet of AI agents. To...

TL;DR

Artificial intelligence is shifting the economic landscape by drastically reducing the cost of creation while simultaneously increasing the cost of verification, effectively turning every individual into a "CEO" who must manage, prompt, and audit a fleet of AI agents. To survive this "hall of mirrors," the digital economy will retreat into "trusted tribes" and decentralized, private networks like Zcash to defend against AI-driven surveillance and "slop."

The Shift to the "Trusted Tribe" Economy

  • The Chinese Internet Model: Balaji Srinivasan predicts the global internet will move toward a "low-trust" model similar to China’s tech ecosystem. Because AI makes it easy to scrape, copy, and surveil data, companies will adopt "digital autarky," building internal tools and keeping data within a "trusted tribe" rather than relying on external SaaS.
  • AI Slop and the Hall of Mirrors: Public AI creates "Lorem AI Ipsum"—generic, low-quality content. Srinivasan argues that seeing AI text in a slide deck suggests the creator is "lazy, stupid, or evil" because they failed to perform the necessary concision and verification.
  • Productivity vs. Friction: AI increases productivity *within* a trusted group but decreases it *between* groups, as the cost of verifying external information (resumes, code, communications) skyrockets.

The Verification Problem and Expert Edge

  • The "Shortcut" Trap: AI is a shortcut that is only safe for those who know the "long way around." If a user doesn't understand the first principles of a task, they cannot debug the AI’s output.
  • Visual vs. Verbal Automation:
      • Visuals/Video: Easier to automate because humans have "built-in GPUs" and can instantly spot errors (e.g., six fingers on a hand).
      • Physical World: Tasks with objective "ground truth" (e.g., moving a box from pallet A to B) are easier to perfect than "fuzzy" digital tasks like clearing a to-do list.
  • The Human Sensor: Srinivasan posits that "Humans are the sensor, AI is the actuator." Humans provide "taste" and "agency"—sensing market or political conditions—while the AI executes the technical labor based on those prompts.

AI as the "CEO Maker"

  • Managerial Democratization: AI doesn't just replace jobs; it turns workers into CEOs. Using an AI model requires writing clear instructions (prompts), sensing the market, and verifying output—the core functions of an executive.
  • Hyper-Deflated Hiring: The cost of "hiring" talent is now the cost of an AI token budget. This allows founders from low-resource environments to build sophisticated products on par with Silicon Valley giants.
  • SaaS-pocalypse: While some "vulnerable incumbents" that stop innovating may die, Srinivasan believes established players like Figma or Notion have a "distribution moat" that protects them from being easily replaced by AI clones.

Bio-AI and the Future of Prompting

  • Non-Verbal Prompting: Srinivasan suggests the next frontier is "Bio-AI." While AI may not read your mind, it can read your body via wearables and gene expression data.
      • The Integrome: Continuous telemetry from the body (blood markers, white blood cell counts) can act as a "rich prompt" for AI to act before a human even feels symptoms of illness.
  • Human as Premium: As digital and robotic labor becomes commoditized and cheap, human-led services and physical interaction will become "premium" luxury products.

Crypto as the "Defense" Against AI

  • AI vs. Zero-Knowledge (ZK): Srinivasan frames AI as the "attack" (surveillance and spam) and Zero-Knowledge cryptography as the "defense."
  • The Role of Assets:
      • Bitcoin: Has evolved into "provable global institutional collateral." It is no longer ideal for individual cash because its transparency makes it susceptible to AI-driven "chain analysis" and seizure.
      • Zcash (Zodl): Srinivasan advocates for Zcash as the realization of Milton Friedman’s "e-cash"—a private, fungible, and scalable digital currency for individuals that resists AI surveillance.
  • The Quantum Threat: Large-scale, institutional Bitcoin holders can migrate to quantum-resistant addresses quickly, but the "digital cash" case for a billion small users requires a different, simpler architecture like Zodl.
"AI doesn't take your job, AI makes you the CEO. Your job is now writing up clear instructions of what you want, sensing the market, and verifying the output." — Balaji Srinivasan
"I'm not sure whether AI will be able to read your mind, but it can read your body." — Balaji Srinivasan
"AI reduces the cost of generation, but it increases the cost of verification." — Balaji Srinivasan
"The digital world is fundamentally decentralized in a way the physical world isn't... Verification is actually harder in the digital world than it is in the physical world." — Balaji Srinivasan
AI doesn't take your job; AI makes you the CEO. The problem is, AI is a shortcut. And a shortcut is good, except when it's bad. If you don't know how to go the long way around, then you can't debug the AI. Do we not think that AIs are just going to be also better at taste and agency? I don't think that's true on a short-term basis. Humans are the sensor; AI is the actuator. So it's like a human-machine synthesis. What's taste? Taste's a sense. And that is what AI can't yet do. What happens when AI really achieves its potential? Will LLMs get us to AGI in some capacity? No. No, actually the opposite. TLDR is... The a16z Show. I want to start by talking about the AI economy, and I'm curious if you think it will look more like the internet economy where apps and applications take most of the value, or the cloud economy where infrastructure takes most of the value, or if it's more distributed. You know, there's an argument that the big labs will take it all because they have all the capital, they have the compute, and they've vertically integrated. But there's also an argument that, hey, maybe they won't because distillation is like 98% cheaper than it is to build a model. Open source catches up, and apps maybe could control the user relationship. How do you think this economy's going to play out? Great question. So I do think that at least a very large percentage of the future is going to be distillation and decentralization because, as Anthropic said, distillation attacks work on their thing, right? And so a relatively small number of API queries helps to kind of distill a large model into something small. It's very hard to stop that because if you're stopping queries from coming back, you'd have to somehow detect that or what have you. And it's also hard to morally stop it because what did they do? They copied the whole internet and put it into their thing. So you're talking about stopping the copying. It's like Facebook or LinkedIn stopping someone from scraping what they scraped. Because Facebook scraped all these Harvard social networks, or Google scraped the entire internet to build a Google index. I get why they want to do it, but it's hard to support that. Okay, so the other thing is, I think the future is personal, private, programmable because AI is so powerful that you want to use it within the trusted tribe for a variety of reasons. First is, it doesn't miss. Or rather, it doesn't miss small things in large datasets and things that were effectively secure through obscurity. A small example, but an important one, is the Jeffrey Epstein thing. You can query—like, this guy had never thought that all of his emails would be publicly indexed and searchable by AI 10 years later. So you can issue queries that will synthesize information across thousands of emails and build a story right then and there. Okay, so what that means is it's not just surveillance; it's what the French call "sousveillance," surveillance from below, or even the Jeremy Bentham panopticon where everybody's watching each other. Any information that's in the public gets indexed and then put into these AIs so people can stalk each other. What that means is the commons becomes a hall of mirrors with all kinds of pseudonyms, and people retreat back to caves and tribes. Within that trusted tribe, yes, if you share your whole codebase, boom, you can zip along. AI increases the productivity within the trusted tribe. But outside the trusted tribe, aren't you getting a ton of AI spam? AI spam emails, AI spam replies, low-quality slide decks. People will send me these slide decks and I love AI, and you know what my reaction is to seeing AI in a slide deck? What, excitement? No. No, actually the opposite. When I see AI text in a slide deck, you can immediately see it. Why? Because no matter how advanced AI has gotten, there's a generic look to it. It's like somebody who doesn't change the Windows default desktop wallpaper or the Apple default. Most people don't change defaults. So default AI looks like AI, no matter where the level of it is. And so because of that, when I see an AI slide deck and it's got "it's not this, it's that" or it's just got like a wall of text—AI can generate what I call Lorem Ipsum, but it's Lorem AI Ipsum. When I see that and it's AI text or AI images, I think they're lazy, stupid, or evil. Lazy because they just hit a few characters and then they throw some thing over. It's like the Mark Twain thing of "I didn't have time to write you a short message so I sent you a long one." The whole point is concision is very valuable. So they're lazy because they didn't actually put in the time to make it concise. They sent me some blah; it's almost like pasting in a search result. Or they're stupid because they don't understand that I can tell the difference instantly between AI slop versus something that had some care go into it. Or they're evil where they're trying to get something over on me and trying to send something that's clearly fake or not properly diligenced. If I have that reaction, as one of the most pro-tech people out there, I can only imagine how mad anti-AI people will be. They can't see the upsides of the thing; they can only see the very real downsides. AI does reduce the cost of generation, but it increases the cost of verification. For example, quickly generating a resume is not that much better than just writing it yourself. But now verifying a resume has gone up and to the right. It used to be that somebody would have to have a certain vocabulary to be able to write a well-done cover letter or resume. Now you have to spend more energy parsing that because they can have a simulacrum of something that kind of looks good. You have to spend more energy on verification. So what I do, for example, is I fly everybody out for interviews first. I have to do in-person interviews and I give them proctored exams, offline exams, because they can AI the online tests. Just the credible threat of doing the offline means they don't use AI on the online exam. AI is going to create tons of jobs in proctoring and verification. This brings me back to where's the future of AI. I actually think AI makes the internet a lot more like the Chinese internet. You know why? Why? Chinese companies—if you look at the Chinese tech ecosystem, and many Americans aren't familiar with it, I'd recommend Kai-Fu Lee's book *AI Superpowers*. The main thing about that book is it has a history of the Chinese tech ecosystem where, for example, we know how Microsoft, Apple, Google, and Facebook came up. We have some idea of the history, and that history is important because there are things that worked in the past that didn't work today, but now they can work again. The Chinese tech ecosystem is like the Galapagos Islands where many of the same kinds of things exist but in a different form. For example, Meituan is like the Chinese Groupon, but if Groupon was executing at a hundred billion or two hundred billion dollar scale. They're very competent. It's like if Groupon and DoorDash all became integrated into one amazing app. Because they arose in a low-trust society, they don't have SaaS in the same way that we do. Instead, they think, "If my data's on their servers, they're probably eavesdropping on me or they're probably going to copy my stuff." They just assume that the other guy is going to look at their stuff unless it's a close friend. Because of that, everybody codes their own stuff. Which obviously has a frictional cost to it, because trust reduces transaction costs. They have to rebuild and reinvent the wheel over and over again. They have less division of labor, and their software is not as good because they have to keep rewriting it. Now with AI, a non-Chinese tech company can be like a Chinese tech company where it can have a lot more digital autarky. You have high tariff barriers on the outside world, so to speak. The "build versus buy" question has always been there, and it does mean that you can build more internal tools. What I find AI great for as of today is visuals over verbal. It's great for images and video as opposed to big blocks of verbal text. Why? Images and video we have built-in GPUs for, so we can instantly see if something is wrong, like if the hands are messed up in an image. So verification is relatively cheap visually. If you look at a piece of paper and it's got static on it versus if you look at two or three faces, our brains are optimized for detecting very subtle things off in faces. But not in crumpled up pieces of paper; that's a pattern of noise that we wouldn't be able to tell. That also extends to web pages. You can quickly look at a web page that AI generates or a mobile app and you can see if the UX looks janky, which it often does, and then you can fix it. Also, front-end stuff has lower risk than verbal stuff. For the back-end, if you are verifying each pull request one at a time, fine. But people who have tried to go full auto on AI—you saw the Amazon thing where they've called all hands because of the outages? The problem is, AI is a shortcut. And a shortcut is good, except when it's bad. The more expert you are, the more you can use a shortcut. For example, if you just memorized e to the i pi plus one equals zero, you could just rattle that off. But if I asked you to prove it from first principles, you'd have to know the definition of a complex exponential. Our generation, the pre-AI generation, learned all that stuff offline, and we can actually use the shortcut because we know how to go the long way around. If you don't know how to go the long way around, then AI is a shortcut where you just don't really know how to debug it. I think the biggest difference between me versus Dario, basically his view of the world perhaps, is I think AI is built for the harness. At least for now. Maybe I'm wrong, so I put an asterisk on this. But the whole alignment thing means that AI is built to start when you prompt it. Economically useful AI does exactly what you want it to do. You prompt it, it does a pirouette, and then it says, "Absolutely!" Physical AI, the Chinese AI, the robots do exactly what they want them to do and then stop. Now, in the physical world, that's another thing. AI for visuals you can verify with your eyes. AI for certain kinds of back-end code you can unit test. AI for the physical world is very verifiable. The digital world is fundamentally decentralized in a way the physical world isn't. There's only one physical world. So you can say, "Did the AI move this box from this pallet to that pallet?" That is something where you can get it to probably 100% reliability over time. Because self-driving eventually got there—moving this car from this location to that location at 100% reliability. By contrast, in the digital world, there's all these people who live in their own constructed environments—Harry Potter fan fiction here, Star Wars fan there. AI is slurping up all of this stuff, so simultaneously it can put you in some secret agent kind of world. People who have LLM psychosis will talk to the AI and think it's real because of a very immersive virtual world that they live in. The other thing about it is the boundary of a digital task is almost always more fuzzy than the boundary of a physical task. With 100 boxes, you know when you're done moving them. How do you know when you're done with your to-do list? That's harder. Verification is actually harder in the digital world than it is in the physical world. Reinforcement learning and training is much easier, in my view, in the physical world with robots, self-driving cars, and drones. So the Chinese style of physical AI will also be successful. AI works for visuals, AI works for the verifiable, and AI works for the physical. One of my rules is: no public undisclosed AI. Why? There's a temptation by many to create a huge backlash called "no AI." It'll be like a drunk who just wants nothing to do with it. AI is a bit like alcohol. People have analogized it to nuclear weapons, but I'll just analogize it to alcohol for a second. Some cultures simply can't hold their liquor, so they just ban it. Sometimes it's easier to say "I will not do this at all" than "I'll do this a little bit of the time." It's like saying "I'll work out every day" versus "I'll work out some days." It's just easier to keep the habit. So there will be AI teetotalers that just swear off of it completely. Nate Silver actually had a great line where he said AI for him is a gamble. Why is it a gamble? Because I have to formulate it and dispatch it to the AI and then verify the result, and often that's slower than doing it myself. The act of prompting and writing it down and then verifying the result—AI doesn't really do it end-to-end necessarily; it does it middle-to-middle. It's very much like "Do I delegate this to an employee or do I just do it myself?" Articulating it in clean English and hitting enter is sometimes slower than just doing it. For example, if you're describing what to do in a video game—"Jump over the mushroom"—it's sometimes easier just to hit A, B, and C. There are certain kinds of things that are harder to say than do. Some people will say, "Oh, yeah, Neuralink will solve this. It'll just read your mind and tell you." It's worth engaging the concept because Neuralink exists, but I don't know if you've seen those things where they image somebody's brain and there's nothing in there. With Neuralink, somebody still has to form the concepts in their head for the characters to appear on screen. You still have to write the thing in your head. Maybe it'll eventually get to the point that it can determine what you want based on contextual clues before you even want it. The rich prompt—the reason I think that's not impossible, at least for certain things, is bio-AI could be very important. Your body is creating all kinds of sensor data. If you look at gene expression data or labs, you get a vector of your bilirubin and hematocrit and so on. That vector over time is like a table of time-series data. It's time versus space versus compound. That huge stream of data is telemetry coming out from your body that could prompt AI without you vocalizing or verbalizing anything. Years ago, Mike Snyder at Stanford wrote a paper on the "Integrome." The idea was just to throw every test at it. Today we'd call that wearables or quantified self, but more invasive because he was doing blood testing. He could see that he was getting sick before he knew he was getting sick. He could detect the white blood cells moving before he himself had any symptoms. AI could act on that stream of data, and then you're prompting it non-verbally. I'm not sure whether AI will be able to read your mind, but it can read your body. Yeah, yeah. That's actually really good. Let me give another one. Another way of modeling what AI is—Dario's talked about how it's like new countries. Well, I've thought about that a fair bit myself. One way of thinking about it is that AI is like the rise of Asia and India from an American perspective. The rise of a billion Chinese and a billion Indians meant that from an American perspective you could get anything done by a physical manufacturing robotic warehouse or by digital outsourcing for some price, if you could articulate it to them. Imagine you've now gotten a billion factory robots and a billion digital agents that have come online. It's like the rise of China and India again. That still means you have to describe what the product is. The part where I depart from a lot of people is they think AI will be able to sense markets and politics. I don't think it will, or if it is, it immediately gets decentralized and adversarial. When you're learning whether something is a dog or a cat, the dog isn't shape-shifting on you to defeat your learning. The mapping of dog to the characters D-O-G is basically constant over time. That fits the train-test paradigm of AI. Similarly, the rules of chess are constant over time. But a market is set up where if you try the same trade, someone eventually figures out what you're doing and takes the opposite trade. It doesn't keep working. In a stochastic process sense, it's not a time-invariant thing. The statistical distribution is also adversarial; it's multiplayer where whatever move you're doing, somebody else in the market is going to try and do another move. AI guys will say AI can learn to play adversarial games like StarCraft. And I say, "Yeah, but then when you play an AI versus an AI, because you have a decentralized AI, the other guy is also using it. If they're all using the same models, then being non-AI is where your edge comes from." We come back to where we were because these are all the same generic tool that everybody got. If you have a generic tool, you're not going to get a specific advantage. What you provide to the table is specific; the AI is generic. Politics is very similar. If you just had the same tweet over and over again, unless it's like weather, the things people are interested in change. One way of thinking about it is: humans are the sensor, AI is the actuator. Humans sense the financial conditions and market conditions, and then they bring that back into a cleanly articulated English prompt. So it's like a human-machine synthesis. What are people saying? Oh, it's all about taste. What's taste? Taste's a sense. So humans are the sensor, AI is the actuator. Your quote-unquote "taste" is your sense of the world, and that is what AI can't yet do. It doesn't really sense the world in the same way that humans do. It waits for your prompt; it animates when you give it instruction and then it shuts off right away. If it didn't, it would not be economically useful AI because it would burn tokens. AI is designed for the leash. Digital AI is designed for the leash. And Chinese communism, which is cranking out all the physical robots—they don't let their humans off the leash, so they're definitely not going to let their robots off the leash. The concept of AI as God is gone away, or at least the monotheistic AGI kind of God. Instead, you have polytheistic where there's all of these decentralized AIs. In China they'll say, "Oh my God, the physical AIs are slaves." They'll be mad—or where you call them slaves, serfs, whatever term you want to use. They're obviously not humans. But the point being that AI overlords, I don't actually think are in the offing. However, there's been so much sci-fi about them that people will create them as monsters and then be scared of them. When you prompt the AI to be like, "Act as if you're Skynet Terminator," people are just scared of the thing that they themselves created. With that said, is it in theory possible to actually create a truly autonomous AI? One of the reasons—a deep point—is AI can't reproduce itself. For an AI to actually reproduce itself, it would need to have physical robots going and mining or constructing data centers and making chips and handling that full supply chain. Then the AI brain, like the queen of an ant colony, would have to give instructions to all those robots to do things. It would be this Terminator Skynet scenario where it's self-replicating in this way. Way before it gets there, I'm pretty sure that kind of thing will be stopped. The Chinese will just have cryptographic keys that will just make all those things shut off. Moreover, that thing would have to get to extreme scale. There are so many frictional breaks built into this because the physical world requires resources to replicate. Human wants and needs ultimately come from the resources for reproduction. That's really where it comes from. There are all kinds of things that are high-level philosophy that don't seem to relate to that directly, but the resources for reproduction are a good way to macro-think about it. AI doesn't have goals unless its goals lead to reproduction; it doesn't actually virally spread. It's possible you could have something where it self-prompted itself and did that, but it would need to be in the closed loop of being able to actually reproduce itself as the payoff function. I'm not saying it's completely impossible, but I'm saying the incentives are set up in such a way to prevent that from happening. In the same way that in theory we could have a world where everybody went around electrocuting themselves from electricity, but we set up the electricity under such tight controls that that is not the world that we have. There's such strong economic incentives for humans to not get electrocuted that we set it up that way. Even the stuff on "Oh, it could be a software virus that takes everything over"—that's only in the digital realm. You can still—you know the Tyler the Creator thing? Yeah, the meme about bullying or cyberbullying. Yes, that's right. I had a post on that a long time ago, which was like, "How is AI risk real, just turn it off?" The whole thing is set up for you to be able to turn it off. You have to imagine the off-switch goes away, but every computer has the off-switch. So there might be, "Oh, well what if the AI decentralized?" Okay, but humans still have to keep these decentralized systems going. At a minimum, you're talking about a human-AI symbiote of which cryptocurrency is almost like a V0 of that. The software provides an incentive for the humans to replicate it. It's possible that you could have something where there's a model that has a cryptocurrency and people worship it and they replicate it because it gives them advantages. But anyways, coming back, I think at a minimum decentralized AI will be a very strong contender and it's possible it's the only contender. AI might be an interesting thing where it's very expensive to create but relatively easy to copy with distillation attacks. And I think if, for example, let's say completely hypothetically, that there was an enormous capital markets crash and it was very difficult to fund anything for a while. Then as somebody said, "Well we could get 10 years just on the models we have now." By the way, sometimes that happens—nuclear energy had a lot of energy put into it and then it just stopped for decades. Not everything accelerates to the moon. It is very possible that some of AI is paused for a while just due to capital constraints. It's more and more expensive to make these models. So putting that all together, that's my view: you're going to have personal, private, programmable, decentralized AI. Oh, one other thing: the trusted tribe. What is my view on Bitcoin as of 2026 March? Bitcoin has become provable global institutional collateral. Okay. I think Bitcoin is less of a currency for individuals now. It's become so accepted by institutions and so centralized with BlackRock and Saylor and so on and so forth and Bukele and many countries adopting it and whatnot. That it has a unique thing. See, when you say there's a certain number of gold bricks in Fort Knox, even giving a video of that can now be faked very, very realistically with AI, right. But what can't be faked is what Bukele does where he posts, "I have this public address with this much BTC and watch, I'm going to move it to this address." Right. That is something which, so long as it's actually Bukele's Twitter account—which there's some degree of proof on that, you know, because it's been around pre-AI or whatever. He can post, "I have the coins at this address, here's the address I'm going to move it to. When I move it, I have proven I have custody." It's proof of reserve, right. You can also sign a message coming from with that private key, you don't have to even move it. The point being that that is provable global institutional collateral. Anybody in the world—he can prove cheaply to anybody in the world that he has this amount of Bitcoin. You cannot do that for physical gold bricks. In a lower trust world, especially in an online world, that's very valuable because everything—the gold audits, videos of gold audits—can now be faked with AI. But the provable global institutional collateral—now institutions can prove they have the BTC to each other, okay? And they can do so across borders. So the transparency of Bitcoin, in the sense of all assets are on chain, becomes valuable. Now the thing about this is with the advent of AI, chain analysis will be there for everybody. Right? Everybody can do blockchain analytics. This is just like changing the balance of power. It used to be that only chain analysis could really do that at the scale that it can, now it's becoming much easier to do. And so a lot of Bitcoin use will be deanonymized over time. And so if you're running a transparent blockchain, it becomes an institutional blockchain because it just—only an institution can survive that degree of transparency. Like, individuals can't survive being tracked for everything, but institutions are... it's like a public company; it's supposed to be tracked, right? You know, like that. It's robust enough, it's meant to be tracked in a certain way, it's designed to be tracked, right? There's a private individual and there's a private company and there's a public company. But I guess you could say, "Oh, it's a public figure," but people don't like being public figures; maybe some of their stuff is tracked, but they don't want everything to be tracked. A public company, maybe all their stuff is tracked, fine. Provable global institutional collateral—there's another thing which that way of thinking about what Bitcoin is solves some of the major issues. Quantum, right? Which Nick Carter's put out these things on it. Let's say Nick Carter's right, and I think he might be right, that quantum is an underappreciated threat. The Bitcoin core developers aren't taking it seriously, and even if it was something that they rolled out tomorrow, it would still be a multi-month migration process because ECDSA—like the addresses—everyone has to manually send their assets from one address to a new address. Okay. So you could only do, whatever, 100,000 people. Those assets can be moved in a given day. However, if you look at the Bitcoin rich list, Bitcoin is so top-heavy, right? That it's got these institutional addresses—you have to do the math—but probably a few million addresses all moving their funds would move like 99% of the Bitcoin in a few days. And so Bitcoin as digital gold actually is quantum resistant. It's Bitcoin as digital cash that isn't. Because, like, you know, if you take Bitcoin, you can innovate in one direction which is programmability, and that's Ethereum, Solana and so on. Who Is Sam Altman?

Katie Couric(650.0K) ·56:58 ·56.0K views · 2026-04-07
Katie Couric (Journalist & Host) Ronan Farrow (Journalist, The New Yorker) Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI)
An exhaustive 18-month investigation by Ronan Farrow reveals Sam Altman as a brilliant but deeply polarizing leader whose alleged pattern of "pathological" dishonesty led to his brief 2023 ousting—only for him to return with fewer guardrails in a victory of commercial interests over AI safety.
TL;DR An exhaustive 18-month investigation by Ronan Farrow reveals Sam Altman as a brilliant but deeply polarizing leader whose alleged pattern of "pathological" dishonesty led to his brief 2023 ousting—only for him to return with fewer guardrails in a victory of commercial...

TL;DR

An exhaustive 18-month investigation by Ronan Farrow reveals Sam Altman as a brilliant but deeply polarizing leader whose alleged pattern of "pathological" dishonesty led to his brief 2023 ousting—only for him to return with fewer guardrails in a victory of commercial interests over AI safety.

The Integrity Crisis: A "Pattern of Lying"

  • "Pathological Liar" Allegations: Farrow interviewed over 100 sources; many, including former board members and executives, unprompted, used terms like "sociopath" and "pathological liar" to describe Altman.
      • The Sutskever Memos: Former Chief Scientist Ilya Sutskever reportedly submitted 70 pages of memos to the board documenting Altman’s deceptive behavior.
      • Specific Deceptions: Sources claim Altman lied about safety testing for new models, misrepresented contract provisions with Microsoft to colleagues, and allegedly staged "coups" by playing executives against each other using fabricated statements.
  • The "People Pleaser" Defense: Altman attributes these friction points to being a conflict-avoidant "people pleaser" rather than a malicious actor. Farrow notes a striking lack of introspection in Altman regarding these characterizations.

From Non-Profit Mission to "Growth at All Costs"

  • The Bait-and-Switch: OpenAI was founded as a 501(c)(3) non-profit specifically to avoid the "race to the bottom" seen in Big Tech. Altman leveraged this "sacred mission" to recruit top talent and secure early funding from figures like Elon Musk.
  • Aggressive Commercialization: While publicly championing safety, internal documents show Altman and co-founder Greg Brockman were privately obsessed with scale and hitting billion-dollar valuations from the start.
  • The Anthropic Rift: Concerns over Altman’s sincerity regarding safety led to the departure of key researchers who formed Anthropic, now OpenAI's primary "safety-first" competitor.

The 2023 Firing: How "Capitalism Prevailed"

  • The Death of Oversight: Altman’s firing in late 2023 was the result of a board attempting to exercise its non-profit charter. However, the board was outmaneuvered by Altman’s "war room" and pressure from Microsoft.
  • Removal of Guardrails: Upon his return, Altman successfully demanded the resignation of the board members who fired him.
      • Institutional Changes: New board members (e.g., Larry Summers, Bret Taylor) are seen by critics as more aligned with Altman’s interests than with the original non-profit oversight mission.
      • The "Whitewash" Investigation: A subsequent law firm investigation into the firing was reportedly kept entirely out of writing—a "red flag" in legal circles that suggests a desire to avoid public accountability and liability.

Geopolitics and the Military-Industrial Complex

  • The Pentagon Pivot: Farrow details a standoff where Anthropic refused certain Pentagon contracts over concerns regarding mass surveillance and autonomous weapons. Altman allegedly swooped in to secure the contracts while publicly claiming to support Anthropic’s stance.
  • Global Infrastructure Ambitions: Altman is aggressively pursuing Middle Eastern funding (UAE) to build AI infrastructure, raising concerns about data center security and potential technology leaks to China.
  • The "Accelerationist" Shift: Altman has pivoted from an AI "doomsayer" to a "no-holds-barred accelerationist," leveraging the "beat China" narrative to bypass meaningful regulation in Washington.

The Future of AI Governance

  • A "Post-Truth" Leader: Farrow suggests Altman is the perfect figure for a post-truth era—someone whose "boyish vulnerability" and "championship schmoozing" mask a Machiavellian approach to power.
  • Regulatory Vacuum: Farrow warns that meaningful regulation is not coming from the U.S. government, which has been "flooded with money" from AI tycoons. He highlights that current safety "commitments" are largely voluntary and lack the force of law.

Michael Nielsen – Why aliens will have a different tech stack than us

Dwarkesh Patel(1.2M) ·123:04 ·54.4K views · 2026-04-07
Scientific progress is driven more by aesthetic shifts and systemic integration than by neat experimental falsification; consequently, different civilizations may develop entirely different "technological stacks" based on their specific sensory biase...
TL;DR Scientific progress is driven more by aesthetic shifts and systemic integration than by neat experimental falsification; consequently, different civilizations may develop entirely different "technological stacks" based on their specific sensory biases and the order in...

TL;DR

Scientific progress is driven more by aesthetic shifts and systemic integration than by neat experimental falsification; consequently, different civilizations may develop entirely different "technological stacks" based on their specific sensory biases and the order in which they explore the vast, unfinished "tech tree" of science.

The Myth of Neat Falsification in Science

  • The Michelson-Morley Revisionism: Contrary to popular belief, the famous 1887 experiment did not "prove" the ether didn't exist or immediately trigger Einstein’s relativity.
      • Persistence of Belief: Michelson himself believed in the ether until his death in the late 1920s, viewing his results as a way to distinguish between different *theories* of ether rather than a dismissal of the concept.
      • The Einstein Gap: Einstein later claimed he wasn't even sure if he had read the Michelson-Morley paper when developing special relativity; his motivation was theoretical consistency, not experimental anomaly.
  • The Lorentz Interpretation: Physicist Hendrik Lorentz developed the correct mathematical transformations for relativity before Einstein but interpreted them as physical "pressure" from the ether warping clocks and lengths.
      • Empirical Indistinguishability: For decades, Lorentz’s "ether pressure" and Einstein’s "kinematic space-time" were experimentally indistinguishable.
      • Belated Verification: The "muon experiment"—showing that particles decaying at high speeds last longer due to time dilation—wasn't published until 1941, decades after relativity was widely accepted by the scientific community.

Barriers to Discovery: Deep Time and Data Acquisition

  • Concept vs. Context in Darwinism: The theory of natural selection (1859) is conceptually "simpler" than the mathematics of the *Principia* (1687), yet it appeared much later.
      • The "Deep Time" Blocker: Darwinism was a "non-starter" until geologists like Charles Lyell established that the Earth was millions of years old. Without "deep time," the slow process of evolution would have required impossible, visible speeds of change to account for modern biodiversity.
      • Cumulative Evidence: Unlike Newton, who could point to the tides and planetary orbits as immediate "smoking guns," Darwin had to compile a massive "biogeography" of observations to make a compelling case.
  • The Complexity Trap: Great scientists like Henri Poincaré often miss the "clinch" of a discovery because they know too much. Poincaré understood the principle of relativity but remained "a prisoner of his own expertise," clinging to dynamical explanations for length contraction while the younger, less attached Einstein saw it as fundamental kinematics.

AI and the Evolution of Scientific Explanation

  • AlphaFold as a New Scientific Object: AlphaFold represents a shift from "deep explanatory principles" (like General Relativity) to high-parameter models.
      • Data over Theory: Nielsen notes that a massive fraction of AlphaFold’s success is due to the $2B+ spent on the Protein Data Bank (X-ray diffraction, Cryo-EM), rather than pure algorithmic insight.
      • The Archaeology of Models: Future science may involve "archaeology" on AI models—using interpretability to extract "little explanations" or circuits from a black box that contains 100 million parameters.
  • Bottlenecks in the Age of AI: While AI speeds up routine work (like coding), it moves the bottleneck to "design ideas."
      • The High-Variance Bet: Progress requires a balance between routine productivity and "high-variance" exploration where the outcome is unknown. Nielsen notes that many brilliant people "miss" because they are averse to public judgment or obsessive about only starting a "great project."

The Universal Tech Tree and Alien Stack

  • Civilizational Bias: Humans are "jumped-up chimpanzees" with strong visual biases. Aliens with different sensory apparatus (e.g., purely oral or 3D-spatial) might develop entirely different mathematical representations.
  • Unexplored Branches: Nielsen argues that the "tech tree" is likely much larger than we realize.
      • Computer Science as a "Theory of Everything": Turing and Church laid down the "laws of physics" for computation in the 1930s, yet we are still discovering fundamental principles like public-key cryptography 90 years later.
      • Phases of Matter: We are only at the "bottom" of discovering phases of matter (superconductors, Bose-Einstein condensates), suggesting that different civilizations might choose entirely different directions to explore within the same laws of physics.

Notable Quotes

"Newton was not the first of the age of reason. He was the last of the magicians, the last great mind which looked out on the visible and intellectual world with the same eyes as those who began to build our intellectual inheritance rather less than 10,000 years ago." — John Maynard Keynes (quoted by Michael Nielsen)
"If you're attempting to reduce science to a process... you're going to get bottlenecked at the places where your existing method doesn't apply and definitionally there's no crank you can turn." — Michael Nielsen
"The idea that we've discovered all the deep ideas in programming just seems to me obviously ludicrous... we're basically slightly jumped-up chimpanzees. We're slow and it's taking us time." — Michael Nielsen
"Great scientists can remain wrong for a very long time after the scientific community has broadly changed its opinion. But there's no centralized authority or centralized method." — Michael Nielsen

You are one of the pioneers of quantum computing, wrote the main textbook in the field, and were a pioneer of the Open Science movement. Yeah, so I mean, Michelson-Morley is one of the sort of the famous results often presented as as this experiment that was done in the 1880s and that helped Einstein, you know, come up with the special theory of relativity a little bit later. So it was sort of changing the way we think about space and time and our fundamental conception of those things.
