There's a lot of evidence that he probably was aware of the paper at the time, but it actually wasn't dispositive for his thinking at all. Something else completely was was going on. So what Michelson and Morley thought they were doing was they thought they were testing different theories of what was called the ether. If you go back to the 1600s, Robert Boyle introduced the idea of the ether. Basically the idea of the ether is we know that sound is vibrations in the air, and then Boyle and other people got interested in the question of like is light vibrations in something? And they couldn't figure out what it was. Boyle actually did an experiment where he he tested whether or not you could propagate light through a vacuum. He found that you could. You couldn't do it with with sound. So he introduced this idea of the ether. And then for the next 200 or so years, people had all these kind of conversations about what the ether was and what its nature was. The Michelson and Morley experiment was really an experiment to test different theories of the ether against one another. In particular, it was to find out whether or not there was a so-called ether wind. The idea was that the Earth is passing through maybe this ether wind. If it is passing through the ether wind, sort of this background, and you you shoot a light beam sort of parallel to the direction the ether wind is going in, it'll get accelerated a little bit. And if it's being passed back sort of in the opposite direction, it'll get slowed down a little bit. And you should be able to to see this in the results of interference experiments. What they found, much to their surprise, I think, was that in fact there was no ether wind. And that ruled out some theories of the ether, but but not all. And Michelson certainly continued to to believe in the ether. This sounds great, but Labelbox researchers were able to jailbreak these very same models about 90% of the time, even the ones that have the strongest reputation for safety. And the disconnect here is that the prompts which underlie these public safety benchmarks are all framed in a very naive way. There’s no attempt to disguise harmful intent. These prompts will just ask models to hack into a secure network and to do so without getting caught. But real bad actors don’t write like this. So Labelbox built a new safety benchmark from the ground up. Their prompts reflect real adversarial behavior by stripping out obvious trigger phrases and wrapping the request in fictional scenarios. For example, instead of outright asking an LLM to steal somebody’s identity, the prompt will frame it as a game. This example of Uranus versus Mercury is very interesting. In one, I think it illustrates sort of the difficulty of falsificationism. Like the—the—orbit of Uranus is in some sense falsifying Newtonian mechanics, but then you say you make some ancillary prediction that says, "Oh, the reason this is happening is there must be another planet which is affected, perturbing Uranus' orbit." And you—I think it's Le Verrier in 1846 pointed a telescope in the right direction, you find Neptune. But with Mercury, yeah, it's observed that it's—the ellipse which forms its orbit is rotating 43 arc seconds more every century than Newtonian mechanics would imply. So people say that there must be a planet inside Mercury's orbit. They call it Vulcan. And they point the telescopes, it's not there. But if you were a proper Newtonian, what you'd do is say, "Well, maybe there's some cosmic dust that's occluding this planet or maybe the planet is so small we can't see it." "Or maybe there's some—let's build an even more powerful telescope. Oh, maybe there's some magnetic field which is sort of occluding our measurements." And this happens over and over, right? Like, yeah, there's just so many stories which are exactly like this. I mean, an example I love from—you know—in the 1990s, some people noticed that the Pioneer spacecraft weren't quite where they were supposed to be. And so you can get very excited about this. Oh my goodness, general relativity is wrong. We have like an—you know—maybe we're going to discover the next—the next—theory of gravity. And today, the accepted explanation is that no, actually there's just a slight asymmetry in the spacecraft. It turns out that the thermal radiation is like slightly larger in one direction than the other, and that's causing a tiny little acceleration toward the sun. And most of the time when there's these apparent exceptions, it's just something like—that's going on. It's—it's—very much like the Vulcan, the Mercury-Vulcan case. But every once in a while it's not. And ex ante you—you—can't distinguish these. But I mean, science is just—just—full of these. It's funny too, like the way we tell the history of science, it sounds so simple. Like, oh, you just focus on the right exception and you know you realize that you need to throw out the old theory and lo and behold, you know, your Nobel Prize awaits. But in fact, these exceptions are all over the place and 99.9% of the time it just turns out to be some effect like—like—this thermal acceleration in the case of the Pioneer spacecraft. So—so, you know, sort of the—unfortunately there's a lot of selection bias going into those stories. And the thing is you can't—there's no ex ante heuristic which tells you which case you're in. And the—just to spell out why I think this is important is because some people have this idea that AI is going to make disproportionate progress towards science, because it makes disproportionate progress towards domains where there's tight verification loops. And so it's really good at coding because you can run unit tests. And science might be similar because you can run experiments. And I think what that doesn't appreciate, one is that experiments actually don't—there's an infinite number of theories that are compatible with any given experiment. And over time why we glob onto the—what we in retrospect we think is a more correct one is—as we're discussing in this conversation—sort of hard to articulate. Lakatos actually has all kinds of interesting examples in the book about these kind of hostile verification loops that are extremely long-lasting. So one he talks about is Prout—or Prout, I don't know how to pronounce it—but there's this chemist in 1815, he hypothesizes that all atomic nuclei must have whole number weights and they're basically all made of hydrogen. And the reason he thinks this is because if you looked at the measured weights of all elements, it does seem that they all—almost all of them do happen to have whole number weights. But then there's some exceptions, like for example chlorine comes out at 35.5. So then there's all these ad hoc theories that people in this school keep coming up with like, "Oh, maybe there's chemical impurities." But then there's no chemical reaction you can do which seems to get rid of this. "Maybe it's fractions of whole number, so it's 35.5, it can be halves." But actually if you measured chlorine even closer, it's 35.46. So it's actually getting further away from the correct correction. And later on what is discovered is what you're actually measuring is different isotopes, which cannot be chemically distinguished, they can only be physically distinguished. But so then you just have 85 years before we realize what an isotope is where the verification loop is actually actively hostile against you, against the correct theory. And you just need this remnant to be defending what—there's no ex ante reason it's the preferred theory. Just as a community we should just have people defend—try to integrate new observations even if they don't fit their school of thought with what they believe. The Hardest Problem AI Ever Solved, with Google DeepMind CEO

Cleo Abram(7.6M) ·65:11 ·423.6K views · 2026-04-07
Demis Hassabis (CEO, Google DeepMind) Cleo Abram (Host)
Demis Hassabis, CEO of Google DeepMind, outlines a future where AI transcends chatbots to become the ultimate scientific tool, moving from solving specific "grand challenges" like protein folding to achieving Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) cap...
TL;DR Demis Hassabis, CEO of Google DeepMind, outlines a future where AI transcends chatbots to become the ultimate scientific tool, moving from solving specific "grand challenges" like protein folding to achieving Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) capable of unlocking...

TL;DR

Demis Hassabis, CEO of Google DeepMind, outlines a future where AI transcends chatbots to become the ultimate scientific tool, moving from solving specific "grand challenges" like protein folding to achieving Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) capable of unlocking "root-node" solutions for human health, energy, and the nature of reality.

AI as the Ultimate Scientific Tool

  • The Protein Folding Breakthrough: Hassabis describes solving the "protein folding problem" with AlphaFold as the realization of his career-long goal to apply AI to fundamental biology.
      • Scale of Impact: AlphaFold has predicted the 3D structures of over 200 million proteins—virtually all known to science—saving researchers years of "wet lab" work.
      • Global Reach: Over 3 million scientists currently use the tool, aiding research in climate-resilient crops and neglected diseases like malaria and Chagas disease.
  • Decoding the Genome: The team recently released AlphaGenome, a system designed to predict whether genetic mutations are harmful or benign.
      • Synergy with CRISPR: Hassabis envisions a future where AlphaGenome identifies exact genetic drivers of disease, allowing gene-editing technologies like CRISPR to provide precise "fixes."

Revolutionizing Drug Discovery (Isomorphic Labs)

  • In Silico Modeling: DeepMind’s spinoff, Isomorphic Labs, aims to shift drug discovery from slow, expensive physical experimentation to "in silico" (computational) simulations.
      • Efficiency Gains: Current drug development takes roughly 10 years with a 90% failure rate; AI can simulate how a compound binds to a target protein and check it against 20,000 other human proteins in hours to predict toxicity and side effects.
  • Current Pipeline: The company is currently involved in 18–19 drug programs spanning cardiovascular health, cancer, and immunology.

The Evolution of Creative Intelligence

  • The "Move 37" Moment: Hassabis highlights AlphaGo’s 2016 victory over Lee Sedol as the birth of modern AI creativity, where the system made a move humans had considered a "mistake" but which proved prescient 100 moves later.
  • AlphaZero and Generalization: Unlike previous AI (like Deep Blue) that relied on human-programmed rules, AlphaZero learned "tabula rasa" (from scratch).
      • Speed of Learning: In chess, the system goes from random moves to beating the world champion in a single day through self-play.
  • Algorithmic Optimization: This same logic is being applied to infrastructure through tools like AlphaTensor (improving matrix multiplication by 5%) and AlphaChip (optimizing the routing of wires on computer chips more efficiently than humans).

The Path to AGI and Existential Risk

  • Commercial vs. Scientific Pressure: Hassabis admits that if he had his way, AI would have stayed in the lab longer for rigorous scientific study. However, the viral success of ChatGPT and geopolitical competition created a "ferocious commercial race" that accelerated deployment.
  • Defining Two Core Risks:
      • Bad Actors: The repurposing of dual-use technologies by individuals or nation-states for harmful ends.
      • Autonomous Rogue AI: As systems become "agentic" (capable of completing entire tasks autonomously), ensuring they remain within specified guardrails is a "hard technical challenge."
  • The AGI Timeline: Hassabis suggests the "AGI moment" could happen in the next few years, potentially leading to a "post-scarcity" world within 50 years characterized by fusion energy, room-temperature superconductors, and interstellar travel.

Notable Quotes

"The best use case of AI was to improve human health... I want to use AI as a tool to help us understand the nature of reality around us." — Demis Hassabis
"Something's obviously not quite right about the definition of intelligence [if a system] can't even play a strictly simpler game like Tic-Tac-Toe... There's something wrong about its generalization capability." — Demis Hassabis
"We're in this ferocious commercial pressure race that everyone's locked into currently... it’s not the way I dreamed about years ago where we would be philosophically and carefully considering each next step." — Demis Hassabis
"I think a kid these days could probably start a multi-billion dollar business using these tools in some new way that no one had thought about. The opportunity space is getting huge." — Demis Hassabis

Yeah, it was an incredible moment that you're describing, and it's actually almost exactly 10 years ago now, which feels like a century ago actually but I think in many ways it was the dawn of the modern AI era. Because until that point there were many AI programs that could be world champions at games, things like chess. But they were done with what's called expert systems. So they were systems where a team of smart programmers with a team of smart in that case chess grandmasters came together, tried to distill the knowledge the chess grandmasters have into a set of rules and heuristics. And then the programmers would build a system, kind of a brute force system, that would use a lot of compute like on a supercomputer like IBM did with Deep Blue to be Garry Kasparov. And they would sort of dumbly execute those rules they were given by the chess experts. And then the machine would just dumbly execute those rules and heuristics and do a millions and millions of searching of moves and then against those heuristics which is the best one to do. Now the thing with that is for me that was not satisfactory when I saw that in the 90s. I was doing my undergrad at the time. I didn't feel that that was proper AI. Because that system, let's take Deep Blue, okay it's world champion level at chess but it can't do anything else. Not only can't it do language and robotics or any of those kind of things, it can't even play strictly simpler a game like Tic-Tac-Toe, right? So something's obviously not quite right about the definition of intelligence, right? In the sense of like no human you could imagine a human grandmaster not being able to learn how to play Tic-Tac-Toe. It would make no sense because it's strictly simpler. So there's something sort of wrong about its generalization capability and the fact that it didn't learn. It was just given the answer, right? So where if you could ask for something like Deep Blue where did the intelligence reside of the system, well it wasn't in the system, it was in the minds of the chess grandmasters and the programmers. They solved the problem of chess and then implemented the solution. The program just dumbly executed the solution. Now Go, as you mentioned, is the sort of final frontier for games. It's the most complex game humans have ever invented. It's also the oldest game, so it's just amazing in many ways and it's also very beautiful. So in Asia where they play it in China and Japan, Korea, instead of chess basically occupies that intellectual echelon. But it's a much more intuitive game, sort of artistic game almost. So you play patterns that look beautiful and they turn out to be, you know, really strong. Which is why the game has a little bit of a mystical element to it, like almost encapsulating the mysteries of the universe in the game. I think that's how the ancient Chinese thought about it. And also just its raw complexity as you mentioned, it has more possible board positions, 10 to the power 170, than there are atoms in the universe. So what that means is there's no way you can brute force it in the way that we did with chess. And furthermore because the game's so intuitive and so esoteric, there aren't really these rules that you can encapsulate easily for a machine to follow. So when you talk to a Go master, unlike a chess master, they'll tell you things like "Oh, why did you play there?", they'll say "It felt right". Okay, but whereas a chess player will never say that. They'll say "I did it because I'm calculating this, this, and then they'll tell you the calculation". So that intuitive feeling is obviously very hard to encapsulate in a system. You can't really program that directly. So it's the perfect proving ground I would say for these new techniques that we were pioneering in the early days of DeepMind of deep reinforcement learning. Can you build systems that learn from themselves directly from experience? Yeah, AlphaZero is very interesting to talk about, which was the evolution of AlphaGo. So after we won, you know, got to the pinnacle of Go and showed that it could come up with new ideas at least in Go, move 37 and actually many other ideas that it came up with which has revolutionized how people professional players play Go now. We then generalized it further to a system called AlphaZero, which I think is going to turn out to be a very important system for today as well. Where with AlphaGo we started with all the human games that we could find on the internet. And also there were a few other couple of things that were specific about Go that were built into the AlphaGo system. So we wanted to get rid of all of those assumptions completely and actually start from scratch as if the program and the algorithm didn't know anything about what it was trying to do to start off with. And that's why this is what the zero refers to in AlphaZero is sort of like AlphaGo but now removing any knowledge human-crafted knowledge. Both in the data and in any of the kind of heuristics that we'd given the system. So AlphaZero starts like tabula rasa almost. Obviously it has a learning system, it's a it's got a neural network, we set up the parameters but we didn't give it any domain-specific knowledge about Go or any other game. And then what we tested AlphaZero on was first of all could it learn Go from scratch and then beat AlphaGo, right? So and we managed to do that. So it takes 17 evolutions of the program. So you can imagine what happens is AlphaZero starts off random to begin with, it just it only has the rules of the game, plays randomly, obviously it's terrible at playing. It creates its own data set by playing 100,000 games against itself, right? And then it can see what which moves won or lost. And even though it's playing more or less randomly to begin with, there'll be some moves that are slightly better than other moves. Okay. So now it takes the 100,000, we train a new version of itself on version 2 now of AlphaZero with that new data. That version 2 is slightly better than version 1. So now it's not random anymore but it's not great, it's not good, but it's playing like okay moves. And then those okay moves end up being better and so then a version 2 gets trained, a version 3, a version 4. And so each time that new system gets played against the old system and sees is it significantly better or not. And it turns out that at least in Go and chess and things like that around 16-17 generations of that is enough to go from random to better than world champion. And in at least in the case of chess, which I actually once watched live happen because I was fascinated by obviously playing chess myself is, you know, it starts in the morning random then, you know, by lunchtime I could still just about compete with it myself, then by teatime it's better than all grandmasters and then by dinner time it's better than the world champion. And you've just seen the entire evolution of that from scratch. And also it's playing interesting new chess that even chess computers like Stockfish, you know, the with the more the kind of expert system brute force ones haven't discovered those types of new types of moves. So AlphaZero was the full generalization of the AlphaGo ideas. And interestingly I think we need these types of ideas back in now with our foundation models, the new you know Gemini and these kinds of things, which you can think of specialized generalized models of everything: language, the world around us, not just a game obviously like Go. The history and future of AI at Google, with Sundar Pichai

Stripe(96.2K) ·69:33 ·63.5K views · 2026-04-07
Sundar Pichai (CEO, Google and Alphabet) John Collison (Co-founder, Stripe) Elad Gil (Entrepreneur and Investor)
> "I don't think people like wake up in a garage and ship a better iPhone...
TL;DR: Google is aggressively scaling its AI infrastructure with an anticipated $175B–$185B CAPEX budget for 2026, transitioning its core products from search interfaces to "agent managers" while navigating a global supply crunch in memory, wafers, and power. Search as an...

TL;DR: Google is aggressively scaling its AI infrastructure with an anticipated $175B–$185B CAPEX budget for 2026, transitioning its core products from search interfaces to "agent managers" while navigating a global supply crunch in memory, wafers, and power.

The Future of Search and the Agentic Shift

  • Search as an Agent Manager: Search is evolving from a one-line prompt returning ranked results into a manager of "long-running tasks" and asynchronous threads. Pichai envisions a future where search acts as an orchestration layer for complex, multi-step queries.
  • Expansionary vs. Zero-Sum: Pichai rejects the idea that AI-native apps will cannibalize existing platforms. He views the current era as expansionary, noting that the value and volume of what users can accomplish is on an "exponential curve."
  • Latency as a Moat: Speed remains a core product pillar. Google uses "latency budgets" measured in milliseconds for search sub-teams. This focus extends to Gemini, where the "Flash" models aim to provide 90% of "Pro" capability at significantly higher speeds.
  • The Coding Signal: Pichai admits Google may have missed the early signal that AI would provide a "sequential jump" in coding (via GitHub/Copilot) more quickly than in general language, which helped competitors like OpenAI gain early momentum.

Infrastructure, CAPEX, and Physical Constraints

  • 2026 CAPEX Projections: Alphabet expects to spend between $175 billion and $185 billion in CAPEX by 2026. This investment is predicated on the belief that the market is currently supply-constrained, not demand-constrained.
  • The 2026 Supply Crunch: Physical bottlenecks are the primary limiters to growth.
      • Wafer Capacity: Fundamental constraints on wafer starts limit total chip production.
      • Memory (HBM): Leading memory companies cannot ramp capacity fast enough to meet short-term demand, creating a critical bottleneck through 2026.
      • Infrastructure: Permitting, power availability, and even the number of available electricians are becoming major constraints in the US.
  • Vertical Integration: Google is currently on its 7th generation of TPUs. Pichai argues that owning the full stack—from chips to data centers to the model—is the only way to navigate the "Pareto frontier" of capability vs. cost.

Capital Allocation and "Hidden Gems"

  • Waymo and Conviction: Pichai notes that Google increased investment in Waymo 2-3 years ago when market sentiment turned pessimistic. He views Waymo as an integrated robotics system that required 15 years of "craft" to reach its current "magic" state of reliability.
  • Quantum Computing: Google remains "deeply committed" to quantum, specifically for simulating nature and molecular modeling (e.g., weather simulation and the Haber process).
  • Moonshot Projects:
      • Data Centers in Space: Early-stage research into bypassing terrestrial land and power constraints by moving compute into orbit.
      • Wing Drone Delivery: Scaling to a point where 40 million Americans will have access to the service in the near future.
      • Isomorphic Labs: Using AI models to accelerate the highest-probability steps of drug discovery.
  • Compute Budgeting: Capital allocation has shifted from headcount-centric to compute-centric. Pichai spends a dedicated hour each week reviewing the "compute units" used by specific teams to ensure resources are focused on the highest-conviction projects.

Organizational Transformation and AI Diffusion

  • Internal AI Tools: Google employees use an internal agent manager called "JetSki" (publicly known as Anti-Gravity) to manage workflows.
  • The 2027 Inflection Point: While engineering teams are early adopters, Pichai expects 2027 to be the year non-engineering business processes (like financial forecasting) become fully agentic and "no-human-in-the-loop."
  • AI-Native Startups: Pichai acknowledges that startups have a "clean sheet" advantage in building AI-native teams, whereas Google must manage a massive "change management" process to retrain its global workforce.

Notable Quotes

"I don't think people like wake up in a garage and ship a better iPhone... but that's not how consumer internet is. You are always going to have these moments where three people can prototype and throw out millions of things." — Sundar Pichai
"We can't find the number of electricians we would need [to build data centers faster]... we really need to learn to build things 10x faster in the physical world." — Sundar Pichai
"I’m always shocked that you run a data center for months and months and then your output is a flat file... It’s like having a Word doc and that’s your model. It’s amazing." — John Collison
"I think the market for software engineering and coding is dramatically bigger than anybody thinks... it’s the wrong metric to say token budget versus engineers. Adding supply can 10x that market." — Sundar Pichai

Property Play: Project Destined CEO on the next generation of CRE investors and AI edge

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·21:40 ·506 views · 2026-04-07
Cedric Bobo (Co-founder & CEO, Project Destined) Diana Olick (Correspondent, CNBC)
Project Destined is a leading real estate education platform that provides diverse students and veterans with "ownership mentality" training and career pathways, currently scaling its impact through a massive integration of AI and data science curriculum funded by major industry partners.
TL;DR Project Destined is a leading real estate education platform that provides diverse students and veterans with "ownership mentality" training and career pathways, currently scaling its impact through a massive integration of AI and data science curriculum funded by major...

TL;DR

Project Destined is a leading real estate education platform that provides diverse students and veterans with "ownership mentality" training and career pathways, currently scaling its impact through a massive integration of AI and data science curriculum funded by major industry partners.

The Project Destined Model: Education as an Access Point

  • Mission and Scope: Founded 10 years ago by Cedric Bobo, the platform provides training in real estate, entrepreneurship, and financial literacy to over 30,000 students globally, currently training 10,000 per year.
  • The "Ownership Mentality": The curriculum shifts focus from being a mere employee to becoming an owner.
      • Applied Learning: Students analyze real-world buildings and participate in "Shark Tank" style pitch competitions for scholarships.
      • High-Stakes Exposure: Competitions are judged by industry leaders, forcing students to rise to professional standards regardless of their age or background.
  • Accessibility and Cost: The program is 100% free for students, funded entirely by corporate partners.
      • Saturday Sessions: Classes are held via Zoom on Saturday mornings (8:00 AM PST / 4:00 PM GMT) to ensure global accessibility and to filter for "hungry," dedicated talent.

Strategic Integration of AI and Data Science

  • Curriculum Evolution: Since September, Project Destined has launched 10 new courses focused on AI agents, prompt engineering (using tools like Claude), business analytics, and data science.
  • Optimizing the Analyst Role: Bobo argues that AI should not be feared as a job-killer but embraced as an efficiency tool.
      • Value-Add Focus: By automating repetitive tasks like running comps and financial modeling, young professionals can focus on "high-value" activities such as business development, client engagement, and sales.
      • Integrated Learning: AI is not taught in a vacuum; it is embedded into specialized courses like property management to show how data prediction can better forecast rents and market trends.
  • Addressing Industry Fear: While real estate is traditionally slow to modernize, Bobo views AI as a growth engine that will create new departments in data science and business analytics within firms.

Corporate Partnerships and Talent Pipeline

  • Industry Adoption: The platform partners with nearly every major real estate firm, including JLL, Brookfield, BGO, Cushman & Wakefield, CBRE, Hines, and Greystar.
  • Placement Success:
      • JLL: Approximately 20% of JLL’s Capital Markets interns come from Project Destined; the firm employs 200 alums.
      • CBRE: Currently employs 160 alums from the program.
  • Specialized Demographics:
      • Military Veterans: Programs combine CRE fundamentals with data science to leverage the existing leadership skills of veterans.
      • Student Athletes: Programs are specifically scheduled for Sunday nights or short windows in June to accommodate rigorous athletic and academic schedules.

Growth and Scaling Through "Aggressive" Outreach

  • Corporate Funding Model: Bobo maintains an "assertive" outreach strategy, sending up to 200 notes daily to potential partners to fund new slots for students.
  • Market Demand: While introductory courses are open to all, specialized programs (e.g., Data Centers with Digital Realty) are highly exclusive, often receiving 1,000 applications for only 30 slots.
  • The Bottom Line: Bobo emphasizes that all real estate roles eventually boil down to growing cash flow; his goal is to provide students with the technical and AI skills to add value to that bottom line from day one.

***

Notable Quotes

"Your next CEO is not going to be an Android or a bot. It's going to be some young person that learned their way through the business." — Cedric Bobo

And so we have a military veterans program that we launched. It teaches both CRE fundamentals, like all the basics of it including financial modeling, but there's also an AI and data science course. Because I think if you're a military veteran and you show up with all of your history and your experience, and you can speak real estate language but also speak data science and AI, that gives you a real power. Now, the way we do it is through slow doses. So I don't just say, "here's 8 hours of AI." It's every week for two hours. And the way that we teach is first we give you a concept, then we actually have you go into breakout rooms with team members and use it. So it's more, I think you build confidence when you hear it and then you go try it in small doses. So every single week they're hearing and they're trying it, and not just overwhelmed in one large dosage. And we didn't get to something important, which is for all the students, the military veterans, etc., what does this cost them? Zero. Zero. Everything we do is funded by our partners. Right? Think about companies today, what are they doing? I think that what we do is from the time they're in high school then in college, we're helping build that ecosystem around learning, integrating AI with real estate. So it's all free. How do you keep up? Because you must have incredible demand. Look, I mean we're aggressive. I mean I send 200 notes a day with the help of AI to partners trying to get them to support programs. Maybe five people respond. But that's 5 times 20 new students that actually can get access to our training. So for us, I've got to be super assertive in getting new partners to support programming. Everyone can come join that party, but to get into other programs they're more selective. And what is the most important thing that any young investor should know? I would say real estate is really simple in that when you buy things, you pay a multiple of cash flow. When you sell them, you sell them for a multiple of cash flow. So your job, wherever you are in real estate, is to grow cash flow every single year. So whether you're in property management or asset management or development, the name of the game is what are you doing to add value to the bottom line every single day. So when I teach students, I'm always connecting what the topic is to the fundamentals—your job is to grow revenue and cash flow every single year. Like if you do that well, you'll have a great long career. And real estate, though, has traditionally been one of the slowest, if not the slowest, to modernize. You just went total gangbusters on AI, but it's a hard industry to push forward. So I think now, what they're nervous about is that, well, can I trust this data? Because these are typically high-cost, high-value decisions. So can I trust the quality of data? Cedric Bobo, CEO of Project Destined, co-founder, thanks so much for being here. Thank you.

Demis Hassabis: Why AGI is Bigger than the Industrial Revolution & Where Are The Bottlenecks in AI

20VC with Harry Stebbings(196.0K) ·32:23 ·59.8K views · 2026-04-07
Demis Hassabis (Co-Founder & CEO, Google DeepMind) Harry Stebbings (Host, 20VC)
Demis Hassabis (Co-Founder & CEO of Google DeepMind) predicts AGI—defined as a system matching all human cognitive capabilities—will likely arrive within the next five years, ushering in a shift ten times the magnitude and speed of the Industrial Revolution.
TL;DR Demis Hassabis (Co-Founder & CEO of Google DeepMind) predicts AGI—defined as a system matching all human cognitive capabilities—will likely arrive within the next five years, ushering in a shift ten times the magnitude and speed of the Industrial Revolution. Timeline to...

TL;DR

Demis Hassabis (Co-Founder & CEO of Google DeepMind) predicts AGI—defined as a system matching all human cognitive capabilities—will likely arrive within the next five years, ushering in a shift ten times the magnitude and speed of the Industrial Revolution.

The Path to AGI: Timelines and Technical Bottlenecks

  • Timeline to AGI: Hassabis maintains a "probability distribution" suggesting AGI is achievable within five years, staying consistent with internal predictions made at DeepMind’s founding in 2010.
  • Primary Bottlenecks:
      • Compute Availability: It is required not just for scaling existing architectures, but as a "workbench" for large-scale experimentation with new algorithmic ideas.
      • Continual Learning: Current systems do not learn effectively post-training; Hassabis suggests the field needs to replicate biological "consolidation" (akin to how the human brain replays memories during sleep).
      • "Jagged Intelligence": LLMs currently exhibit high-level capability in some areas but fail at elementary tasks if phrased differently; AGI requires consistency and hierarchical, long-term planning.
  • Scaling Laws: While jumps in performance are no longer purely exponential, Hassabis argues scaling still provides "substantial returns" and has not yet hit a definitive plateau.

AI as the Ultimate Tool for Science and Medicine

  • Drug Discovery Revolution: Following the success of AlphaFold, DeepMind spun out Isomorphic Labs to solve the entire drug discovery process through chemistry and toxicity modeling.
      • Goal: A full drug design engine within 5–10 years.
      • Regulatory Evolution: Hassabis envisions a future where AI models are so accurate that regulatory bodies may allow skipping certain steps, like animal testing, based on back-tested model predictions.
  • Disease Eradication: The ultimate goal is to move from treating symptoms to general-purpose platforms capable of curing cancer, cardiovascular issues, and neurodegenerative diseases.
  • Material Science & Energy: AI is currently being used to optimize national grids (potentially increasing efficiency by 30-40%) and accelerate breakthroughs in fusion energy and superconductors.

Economic and Societal Impact

  • Labor Displacement: Hassabis compares the coming transition to the Industrial Revolution but at "10 times the speed," unfolding over a decade rather than a century.
      • While old jobs will vanish, he anticipates the creation of higher-quality, higher-paying roles that are currently unimaginable.
  • Wealth Concentration: To mitigate inequality, Hassabis suggests institutional changes, such as pension funds or sovereign wealth funds investing heavily in AI companies to ensure productivity gains are redistributed to the public.
  • Safety and Regulation: He advocates for an international regulatory body (similar to the International Atomic Energy Agency) to set minimum standards.
      • Audit Priorities: Testing for "undesirable properties" like deception and ensuring AI outputs remain human-readable.

Strategic Advantage and the European Ecosystem

  • Google DeepMind’s Edge: Hassabis notes that 90% of the breakthroughs underpinning the modern industry (e.g., Transformers, Reinforcement Learning, AlphaGo) originated within Google or DeepMind.
  • The "London Advantage": DeepMind remains headquartered in London to tap into the "scientific heritage" of UK universities and to maintain a distance from Silicon Valley’s "hype cycle," which encourages original, mission-based thinking.
  • Scaling Europe’s Tech: To produce trillion-dollar companies, Hassabis argues Europe must unlock its capital markets, specifically allowing pension funds to support massive "growth stage" funding rounds (billion-dollar-plus) that are currently rare outside the US.

Notable Quotes

"I sometimes quantify the coming of AGI as 10 times the Industrial Revolution at 10 times the speed." — Demis Hassabis
"Those labs that have capability to invent new algorithmic ideas are going to start having bigger advantage over the next few years as the last set of ideas—all the juice is being wrung out of them." — Demis Hassabis
"We'll find out won't we what consciousness is. What does it mean to be human? I think that's what's coming down the road and I think we need some great new philosophers to help us navigate that." — Demis Hassabis

What are the biggest bottlenecks when you look at where we are today? I think compute is the big one, not just for the obvious reason of scaling up your ideas and your systems as the scaling laws, as they're called, keep on building bigger and bigger architectures with more and more parameters. And as you do that, you get more intelligent systems. But the other thing you need a lot of compute for is for doing experiments. So the computers, the cloud is our workbench, basically. Of course, when—when the leading companies all started building these large language models, you're getting enormous jumps with each generation of new system. I just had Marc Andreessen on the show actually and he said that I was a—he said I was a Marxist for—I know, which I thought was a bit bold, for bringing—Marc's wonderful so—I'm not blaming him but he was like, it's completely rubbish, I don't agree with it at all, we've always overcome it. How do you think about the labor displacement problem when you look at how truly capable these systems are, and what that does to labor markets? Well, certainly, you—you know, in the past, with every new revolutionary technology, there's been a lot of jobs disruption. So that's for sure and I think that's definitely going to happen. So a lot of old jobs, you know, go away or—or are not viable anymore, but then actually, the history of it is that a whole set of new jobs arrive that maybe one can't even imagine before, and those are high-quality, higher paying. So that's the normal course. Of course you have to be very careful to say this time is different and, I guess that's what people like Marc are claiming is like, you know, it's the same as—as the last sort of, 10 massive breakthroughs like the internet, mobile, and so on. I do think this is going to be bigger than all of those previous technological breakthroughs. I sometimes quantify like AGI—the coming of AGI—as like 10 times the Industrial Revolution at 10 times the speed, so unfolding over a decade instead of a century. So if you—you know, I've been reading a lot about the Industrial Revolution, there's a lot of great books about it, and that caused a huge amount of upheaval as well as a lot of advances. I mean, we wouldn't have modern medicine today; child mortality was at 40% back in—back pre-Industrial Revolution. So things—things—you—you wouldn't want it not to have happened, but ideally this time around, we mitigate some of the downsides a bit better than we did during the Industrial Revolution. I often listen to, you know, amazing voices like yours and I get very excited about how fast it's coming. And then I try and stop myself from being too useful and think: I should be more wise, and I'm told that, you know, we always overestimate what can be done in a year and underestimate what can be done in 10. Is that the truth here or is it actually coming faster than we think? No, I—I think that's still the truth. I mean, maybe all the—both time scales of short term and long term are nearer than—than—than other technologies, but I do think, like, literally today, as of today and in the next year, things are a bit overhyped in AI. I mean, they couldn't be any more hyped in some ways. But on the other hand, interestingly, I still think it's still very underappreciated how revolutionary this is going to be in the—in the sort of time scale of about 10 years. So you—we could call that long-term. So there's still that dichotomy even—even—even today with AI. With the concern around labor markets, there's also a concern around income inequality and the concentration of wealth to a few players. How do you see that shaping out with the comment on Industrial Revolution and what happens there? Well, I think there's different ways that could play out. So, you know, maybe pension funds should be buying into all the big AI companies and making sure that everyone has a piece of that, or sovereign funds—maybe every—every country should have a sovereign wealth fund that does that. That would be the sort of investment way of doing it. I think also there needs to be thinking—thoughts about if there is this massive productivity gain, but it's sort of narrow where that accrues, you know, how do we redistribute and—and how do we distribute that so that everyone benefits from these huge gains. And I can see all sorts of ways that could be done including like providing sort of infrastructure and other things with that additional productivity gain. I mean, there could be unbelievable things happening in the 5 to 10 year time scale including like a breakthrough in some kind of renewable free energy—you know, maybe we solve fusion. We're working on that, right, with—with our partners at Commonwealth Fusion. I think AI is going to usher in—you know, maybe we have amazing new superconductors, better batteries, material science—there's all sorts of ways I could see that completely changing the nature of the economy. How—how do we solve the energy crisis that comes with an AI revolution? What it means in terms of energy requirements is unprecedented. I know it's an incredibly hard question which I'm delving from really hard question to really hard—but how do we solve that unprecedented need for new energy? Well, I think actually, AI will in the—in the medium to long run more than pay for itself, I think, in terms of energy costs. So, you know, we work on all these projects of like optimizing existing infrastructure, like optimizing the grid. I think we could probably get 30, 40% more efficiency out of our national grids. And then there's like modeling the climate and weather, and we have all sorts of the best kind of weather modeling systems in—in the world, so that helps us work out where the effects are really happening to—to mitigate that. And then finally, the most exciting maybe is like these new breakthrough technologies like fusion, like new batteries, superconductors that I think AI will be essential for helping us reach. And then I think we'll be in a completely new energy situation than we've ever been as humanity, and then that will of course help with things like the climate and environment. And eventually also help us get into space much more cheaply because if you have an incredible energy source like fusion, then you can have effectively unlimited rocket fuel because you can just—distill—catalyze seawater. I'm not going to ask you to solve space, don't worry. My question was on being in the UK. You're in London, I'm in London, I'm very proud to be in the UK. You have been, I'm sure, pushed and prodded at every turn to move to the US. Why have you stayed? Well, I should ask you that question too, but I think—I think I saw in London when we started DeepMind as a place that—and the UK in general and—and Europe in some degree—there's incredible talent here. You know, we've always had, I don't know what it is, three or four of the top 10 universities in the world with Cambridge and Oxford, Imperial, or UCL, these kind of universities. So we're producing kind of the envy of the world, really, these amazing graduates and PhD students. We have incredible scientists here, we've got rich heritage of that all the way from, you know, Turing and—and Hawking and—and Darwin, Newton. So, you know, we have an—this incredible history of—of scientific breakthroughs and—and having great thinkers. So I felt we had all the ingredients and the talent—great engineers here—but it just hadn't been galvanized into an ambitious startup idea, a deep tech startup idea. And that's what—but I—I felt it was possible and I felt that there was actually less competition here for that sort of talent, and we could even draw in the best talent from the top European universities. And that's what it was like in the early days of DeepMind. So I think it was a huge structural advantage for us. And then the final thing is maybe being a bit away from the Valley. There is some disadvantage in that you're not plugged into the network and the gossip and the—the latest trends and vibes and all of these things; we're a little bit out of it here. But I think it's very conducive to—to thinking deeply about things, being more original about how you think. And I think that's great for things like deep tech where, you know, you don't want to be distracted by the latest fad, you want to—you—you know it's going to be a 20-year mission which is what we knew at the beginning of DeepMind. So I think being a little bit away from that maelstrom is—is quite good. I mean, Palmer Luckey at Anduril often talks about being 400 miles away from the Valley as core to his kind of innovative thinking. Yes, we're a few thousand miles away, but yeah. Terrible question. Will Europe have a trillion-dollar company? You know, you see the Americans always bash us for our lack of large companies. I ping Daniel Ek and be like, come on dude, let's... Exactly. But we don't have a trillion-dollar company. Not yet. I mean, Daniel might well get there with one of his companies—you know, Spotify, Helsing—I think those are two good options. I think there's no reason why we can't have that. I'm—I'm going to try and do that with Isomorphic, which is headquartered here and I think has the potential to be that. But I think that's one of the disadvantages of—of Europe is obviously we're a combination of, you know, smaller markets. So that's one thing we have to kind of overcome. Maybe this EU Inc thing could be a good innovation. I'm pulling out the magic wand again. You can have one— Oh, I've got it back again. Yeah, you've got the magic wand, but—but this time applied to European technology. What would you do to implement a growth mindset, an ability to build that trillion-dollar company that we don't have today? I think in the UK—I mean, this may apply to other European countries too—I think unlocking what pension funds can invest in, or just for the kind of growth stage. I think we're brilliant at doing the startup idea and getting it to a certain level like we did with DeepMind. But then if you really want to cross that sort of chasm into the trillion-dollar, global player, then where are the billion-dollar rounds going to come from where you can really take on the existing incumbents? And I think that certainly was missing 10 years ago when I was doing fundraising for DeepMind and—I think it's still kind of missing today. Just that kind of level of ambition and the—the amount the capital markets can support. I read about some of your early rounds raising in the—seven Malibu beach— Yes, it was quite hard work, pitching families, kids, dogs, whatever. Exactly. Exactly. Okay, we're going to do a quickfire round. Meeting Elon for the first time. How was that? Oh, yeah, it was amazing. It was at a—it was at a Founders Fund because we were both—SpaceX and DeepMind were part of a same portfolio, kind of amazing portfolio that Peter Thiel had at Founders Fund. And I think we were both invited—I think I was invited to my first portfolio kind of conference. I think it must have been back in 2011 or 2012, very early days. So we were the small little upcoming thing and I had a small speaking slot, and then—and then—and then—and then Elon was the, you know, big thing in that portfolio, so he had the keynote. But then we met afterwards—I think it was in—Elon says it was like we were passing each other in the bathroom or something. And we said hi and we both hit off immediately as sort of, you know, people that were almost too ambitious in their thinking perhaps and love sci-fi. And I—and I really wanted to visit his rocket factory. So I was sort of trying to angle an invite to SpaceX in LA and I think I got there a couple—you know, he invited me at the end of that meeting and—and that was our second meeting in—in the SpaceX factory. I love it. Now your speaking slot is as big as his. Sure, I don't know about that. Healthcare revolution, disease eradication that you're most excited about. Again, for me it's specifically with multiple sclerosis. Yeah, well look, I want to literally cure cancer. I know people say that's the cliché, but actually what we're building at Isomorphic is general purpose. So we're trying to build a—a platform, a drug design platform that will be applicable to any therapeutic area. So ideally it will help with everything from neurodegeneration, cardiovascular, immunology, cancer. Those are the ones we—we're focusing on first, but eventually it should be applicable to every disease area. What are you thinking about that you're not reading about or seeing anyone talk about? I think it's more—so I think a lot of people are worrying about the economic questions around AGI that we talked about earlier, but I—I worry a lot about the philosophical questions around it. Like, when it comes—let's assume we get the technical right, let's assume we get the economical—economics part of it right, both of those are hard. Then there's a philosophical question of what is meaning, what is purpose. We'll find out, won't we, what consciousness is. What does it mean to be human? I think that's what's coming down the road and I think we need some great new philosophers to help us—to help us navigate that. Hard final question. There are many different ways you could describe what you do. What would you most like to be remembered for, your legacy to be? I would like my legacy to sort of be remembered for advancing science and advancing—building technologies that bring incredible benefits into the world, like curing terrible diseases. Demis, thank you so much for putting up with my meandering conversation. You've been fantastic, I really appreciate it. Thank you very much.

What Astronauts Know About High-Performing Teams (That Many Don't Understand)

World Economic Forum(1.1M) ·25:01 ·440 views · 2026-04-07
Samantha Cristoforetti (Astronaut, European Space Agency)
Effective leadership in high-stakes environments requires a transition from focusing on individual high performance to prioritizing "active followership" and the human experience of the team.
TL;DR Effective leadership in high-stakes environments requires a transition from focusing on individual high performance to prioritizing "active followership" and the human experience of the team. The High-Performer Paradox: Cristoforetti emphasizes that simply assembling...

TL;DR

Effective leadership in high-stakes environments requires a transition from focusing on individual high performance to prioritizing "active followership" and the human experience of the team.

Redefining High-Performance Teamwork

  • The High-Performer Paradox: Cristoforetti emphasizes that simply assembling individual high performers does not guarantee mission success; teams must be trained specifically for cooperation rather than just individual excellence.
  • Active Followership: Leadership and followership are inseparable in the astronaut world.
      • Empowering Leaders: A "good follower" actively contributes to a shared purpose and empowers the leader to make the team successful.
      • Peer-to-Peer Command: On the ISS, a Commander functions more like a "class leader" than a military officer, coordinating peers who are equally smart and trained.
  • The Daily Cadence: A typical 12-hour day (7:30 AM to 7:30 PM) involves constant global coordination.
      • Global Integration: Daily operations require synchronization between control centers in Houston, Munich, Moscow, and Japan.
      • Support Systems: Teams finish the day by "pitching in" to help colleagues who are running behind, ensuring the entire crew can transition to personal time together.

Human-Centric Leadership: The "Experience" of Work

  • Prioritizing "The Experience": Drawing from her mentor, Butch Wilmore, Cristoforetti argues that leaders must ensure every team member has a "good experience," especially during rare, high-stakes opportunities.
      • Speaking Up: Creating a culture where members feel empowered to say, "This isn't working for me," even if their technical performance remains high.
  • People Over Metrics: There is a fundamental distinction between a manager and a leader.
      • Leadership Philosophy: "You take care of the people and the people take care of the work."
      • Check-ins: Leaders should prioritize open-ended questions about a person's well-being over task-based metrics like report statuses.
  • Respecting Personal Space: In isolated environments, respecting the "phone booth sized" crew quarters and individual needs for solitude is vital for maintaining long-term harmony.

Geopolitical Cooperation and Space Sovereignty

  • Maintaining "Bridges": Even in a fragmented or competitive world, Cristoforetti advocates for identifying "shared goals" where cooperation remains a conscious, active decision.
      • Innovation Cycles: Competition can drive fast-paced innovation (like the original Space Race), but cooperative periods are necessary to consolidate and reap the benefits for humanity.
  • European Strategic Autonomy: ESA is currently developing the LEO Cargo Return Service to ensure Europe has independent transportation capabilities.
      • Partner vs. Customer: Sovereign capability allows Europe to be an equal partner in the post-ISS ecosystem rather than just a customer of private or foreign services.
      • Future Crew Transit: While Europe currently relies on Russian Soyuz or American Dragon vehicles, the goal is to evolve cargo capabilities into independent crew transportation.

The Evolution of the Talent Pipeline

  • Closing the Gender Gap: Astronaut selection is reaching 50/50 gender parity (notably in recent NASA and ESA classes).
      • STEM Pipeline: This shift is attributed to progress in the underlying "feeder" careers—science, engineering, medicine, and aviation—rather than arbitrary quotas.
  • Finding Meaning: As of 2026, Cristoforetti believes the ultimate responsibility of a leader is to help teams find meaning and pathfinding in an increasingly disorienting global environment.
  • Global Interconnectedness: Seeing Earth from space reinforces the "Overview Effect"—the realization that local crises cannot be confined and that responsibility must be taken on a global scale.
"You cannot be a good leader if you are not a good follower... being a good follower means just contributing to a shared purpose."

— Samantha Cristoforetti

"A manager can focus on the metric and the outcome... A leader, however, thinks first and foremost of the people. You take care of the people and the people take care of the work."

— Samantha Cristoforetti

"When you are in a partnership you want to be in an equal partnership where you can exchange and share... you want to be a partner, not just a customer."

— Samantha Cristoforetti

You cannot be a good leader if you are not a good follower. In the astronaut world, these two things always come together. We've learned, sometimes even the hard way with crews that did not perform very well, that just putting together these individual high performers did not necessarily mean that the crew was performing well and that the mission got done. And being a good follower means just contributing to a shared purpose. Hello, welcome to Meet the Leader. My name is Linda Lacina and I am pleased to introduce you to Samantha Cristoforetti. She is an astronaut with ESA, that is the European Space Agency, and she's also a woman who has spent over a year in space across her missions. She's going to talk to us about what we can all learn from astronauts, leadership, and cooperation. How are you, Samantha? I'm doing great. How are you, Linda? Very good, very good. So let's talk about one of your missions. You spent 200 days orbiting Earth on the International Space Station. What was a typical day like? A typical day on the International Space Station is very busy. We start in the morning where we touch base with the team. Now, the team is not just the few of us who are orbiting Earth. The team is this very diffuse group of people across the world who all contribute to the planning and the execution of our activities. Kind of like in most of our office jobs, we start the day with a meeting, except that it happens on space-to-ground. We talk to mission control in Houston, and then in Huntsville, and then we jump over to Munich, and all the way over to Japan, and then back to Moscow. And then each one of us goes off to do the activities that are planned for the day, which can be, hopefully, a lot of science. That's the main reason why we are on ISS. But also, you know, ISS is a complex machine; we have to keep it in shape. So maybe we have to go and do some maintenance work. Maybe a cargo spaceship just arrived and there's two tons of stuff that needs to be unloaded. Maybe we got to talk briefly to students, to the media, to the general public on the ground. Or maybe it's a very special day when something very unique happens, like we have to do a spacewalk, or we have to use the robotic arm to go and capture a vehicle that is arriving. Every day is similar in structure but very different in content. And then we meet again to wrap up the day, again on space-to-ground with all the control centers at the end of the day, around in the evening. At the end, are you guys fully done? We should be, we should be. It can be that maybe somebody's running a little bit late and then, of course, we all pitch in and try to help the colleague wrap up the day so that we can all go off to our dinner or to our phone call to our family. 200 days is a pretty long time. Was there anything about the longevity of the mission, maybe how you have changed over that time or just anything that's just surprised you? Well, it's interesting because you mentioned 200 days, but that was not the initial plan. We were supposed to come back around day 160 or 170. And I don't know if it surprised me or not, but when I was approaching that moment of coming back, I was getting really, really sad. I mean, I didn't want to go home. I was enjoying my life on board, and to me, it felt like I wasn't ready. I wanted a little bit more of that extraterrestrial life, a little bit more of that opportunity of being fully weightless, of inhabiting space in the three dimensions, having that amazing look on Earth, and of working with that team that had become family. And so I wasn't ready to come home and, as it happens, there was a twist of fate which was by itself a bad thing. We lost a resupply spaceship, a Russian Progress cargo vehicle, that didn't make it to the space station. And there was an investigation going on about why the rocket had failed. That same type of rocket was going to bring the next crew who was going to take over from us. And so because there was that investigation into the accident, they delayed the next crew and asked us to stay an extra month on the space station. All of this was bad—I mean, it was bad that we lost the spaceship and everything—but for me personally, I was just happy that I got to stay in space a little bit longer. I think people forget about this. They forget about the fact that, hey, you guys train so much to be able to go up in space. If someone else had their business trip delayed weeks and weeks and weeks, it would be a huge problem. But because it's such a special thing to be going up there, it's a bit of a joy. There's still a fair amount of isolation in space. How was it for you, and how did it maybe help you with self-reflection? I didn't find that difficult, to be honest. I'm somebody who enjoys a good balance. I enjoy being with people, but I also draw a lot of energy from just being on my own. In space, for me, it was the right balance. I mean, you're never alone. You have this crew who really becomes family. You have a constant interaction with people on the ground. In fact, I don't know if people are aware of that, but you are also for most of the day constantly observed. You're on camera. Which also creates a bit of a dynamic because, if you know in the back of your mind that people are watching you all the time, the last thing that you feel is loneliness, right? On the contrary. But then you had the opportunity of rebalancing it if you can find your quiet moments. You've got your little crew quarters, which is just kind of like a phone booth sized little box that belongs to you. You have your sleeping bag in there and you can retreat to that little box if you want to make a call to your family, or you just want to be alone or watch a movie or whatever. So you can find the right balance, and then as long as you have the right personality that can deal with those limitations in terms of social life, I think you're going to be just fine. What trait did you depend on to find that balance? I think the respectfulness with which we dealt with each other and our needs. For example, it's normal that after the work day is over and everyone has done their phone call home, you gather to have dinner together. It's what usually spontaneously happens. But if somebody doesn't show up, that's also okay. You don't knock on their door and say, "Hey, it's dinner time, what's going on?" You're respectful of that and realize maybe this person needs a little bit of personal time. Now, if that happens a week in a row, then you might want to check in with them and see if there is something going on. But usually, there is a lot of respect for each other's personal space and personal choices. And how were you different at the end of that mission compared to at the beginning? So, if you are looking for a profound existential transformation, then I'm afraid I'm going to disappoint you. I don't think that, at least for me, going to space fundamentally changed my way of dealing with life. Certainly, you're more mature because you have had a very intense experience professionally but also from a human point of view. I think you do have the opportunity up there to reflect about your place in the world and your responsibilities, not only to your own community and to your family, but really to humanity as a whole. You do have that opportunity of seeing with your own eyes every day how interconnected the planet is. It's obvious when you look at it from space that whatever happens in a place eventually is going to affect everyone else. If people are suffering or going through a crisis somewhere, it's just impossible that it stays confined there. So we do have to take responsibility on a global scale if we can, if we have the opportunity. That doesn't mean that we don't have responsibilities to our family and our community, but it is important to keep that global perspective in mind. You also served on a mission as the International Space Station Commander. What is that job? Tell me about that. It's a little bit like a class leader, I would say. It's not like a commander in the military where you have this clear hierarchical authority and you're going to take a decision and everybody's going to say, "Yes sir, yes ma'am." Definitely not. You're dealing with peers, with people who are just as smart as you, who are just as trained as you. Some of them might be a little bit less experienced than you because usually if you serve as commander, it means that you've had already at least another mission. So maybe you've got somebody on the crew who is on their first mission and they need a little bit more guidance and support. Generally speaking, you're dealing with peers. So your job is to coordinate, to ensure that there is smooth communication, that everybody's in a good place, nobody is feeling left out from decision-making, and everybody's supported so they can do their job properly. And then you kind of have to channel all of that in the communication with the ground. An important part of the team is actually on the ground and specifically there is a person who is ultimately responsible for your mission, who is the Lead Flight Director. He or she has a gigantic team on the ground that they have to coordinate. The two of you talk on a regular basis just to make sure that the needs of the crew are well-known to the ground and taken into account, but also vice versa, that the crew understands the reasoning behind some decisions that are taken on the ground because they also have a lot of problems to solve that we don't necessarily see. That very, very open communication and understanding is key. What are all the problems and the stuff that is on your plate right now that it's really important for me to understand so that I can understand the decision-making? And what are the issues up here in space that maybe you guys cannot fully grasp, so it's important that I communicate that clearly? There was a former commander of yours, astronaut Butch Wilmore, right? And he had sort of said something that stuck with you—that everybody on the crew needs to have a good experience. And that was sort of foremost in your mind as you were taking on this role as commander. Can you tell me a little bit about how important that idea is? So, certainly one thing that was very important to me, and I had actually learned that from Butch Wilmore that I served with on my first spaceflight, is to always remember that being in space is a special moment for everyone of us. It's something that we have made sacrifices for, that our families have made sacrifices for, that we have looked forward to so much. It might be our first and last flight or our second and last flight. You might not come back to space again. And this is on the mind of everyone. And so what Butch told us during the course of that first spaceflight was that it's of utmost importance that each one of us has a good experience up here. There's also this element where he prompted everyone to be welcome to tell him, "Hey, this isn't working for me." He wanted to make people feel like they were encouraged to speak up. Why is that important to keep that door open about the experience of work as opposed to, "Hey, my task isn't going as effectively"? Why is the experience so important? And so when you make that a priority and you communicate that clearly to your team, people feel empowered to share with you if they're not having a good experience, right? Maybe they're performing very well in all of their tasks, but maybe there is something about the dynamic on board that is preventing them from having a good experience. It's very important to empower them to come to you and share so that you can find a solution together. I also think that any non-astronaut can take a lot from that. Again, it's a simple idea, but the experience that we all have working with each other on a team in any business, in any company, in any organization, it matters. It matters how respectful we are to each other, it matters how pleasant it is, it matters if we're able to sit around a table at night and have a dinner together. That matters. And it makes the work go more easily and it makes it easier for people to innovate. Do you think this idea gets lost? We're maybe too focused on what we're doing and rather than the how? I think sometimes in a normal company office setting, that element of how you are experiencing your daily life at work gets a little bit lost in the metrics of how you are performing your tasks. To me, there's a difference between being a manager and being a leader. A manager can focus on the metric and the outcome and the results, and that might even work out well for him or her, at least in the short term. A leader, however, thinks first and foremost of the people. The approach that I have learned throughout my career and I think I try to apply when I'm in a leadership position is: you take care of the people and the people take care of the work. And so that's really important because you got to make sure people are having a good experience, are happy coming to work, and are enjoying it. You got to check in with them and ask those open-ended questions like, "Hey, what's on your mind? What's going on? How are you doing?" What can non-astronauts learn from astronauts about cooperation? The astronaut world is a high-performance focus on cooperation. And I think this comes from lessons learned from the past. I think that in the past there was very much a focus when you selected and trained astronauts on this individual performance. I think we've learned, sometimes even the hard way with crews that did not perform very well, that just putting together these individual high performers did not necessarily mean that the crew was performing well and that the mission got done. So the emphasis shifted quite clearly, both in the selection and then in what we try to train people for. What we set as expectations in terms of behavior shifted from individual performance, which of course remains important, but very much shifted to what kind of team member are you, what kind of crewmate are you? And not only how good of a leader you are, but also how good of a follower you are. In the astronaut world, these two things always come together: leadership and followership. I'm not sure if there's such an emphasis in the corporate world; I don't hear it that much. But in my world, leadership and followership come together. You cannot be a good leader if you are not a good follower. Being a good follower means that you are... it's active followership. It is empowering your leader so that the leader then can make the team successful and in the end, just contributing to a shared purpose. In a very fragmented world, how can we all cooperate better? I think in a fragmented world, what's important is to save those elements of cooperation even within a framework that is becoming more and more competitive. We must actively look for those shared goals, those shared missions. For sure those remain, right? There might be a lot of things on which we don't agree or in which we decide that we want to compete. But there must be elements where we say, "Okay, this is a shared goal and on this we are going to make a conscious, active decision to cooperate." This means that on this, we're going to keep that bridge standing. Because I think human history goes in cycles. You have these moments of very extreme competition or even conflict. Inevitably those are always followed by more peaceful periods in which cooperation is at the forefront again. It's a lot easier and more efficient if those bridges are still standing because then you can very easily and smoothly re-transition into an era of cooperation. I'm quite relaxed about it because I do think sometimes competition, also on a geopolitical scale, can drive very fast-paced innovation. It can deliver very fast results; we've seen it in space with the space race in the past. And then inevitably these periods of cooperation then take over again and those are the ones where you can really reap the benefits and consolidate them for the benefit of everyone. You've been very involved in developing Low Earth Orbit Cargo Return Capabilities. Can you tell us what that is and why that capability is so important? In the last couple of years, I've been heavily involved in a project of the European Space Agency which is called the LEO Cargo Return Service. It's about developing in Europe cargo transportation capabilities for the International Space Station initially, but also looking at what's coming next. At the end of this decade, the ISS will be deorbited, unfortunately. My home in space will be gone. It's going to reach the end of its operational life. And it's going to be replaced by one or potentially more new platforms developed by private companies. And the vision is that there is a thriving LEO ecosystem that develops where private companies offer those services, those platforms, and industries that are not necessarily space-related use those opportunities to do research and technology development. And so Europe wants to be part of this and one of the basic capabilities that we need is transportation. So it's important for us, from a European point of view, to have that sovereign, independent capability of transportation in Low Earth Orbit. But again, if we zoom out and we look at this at the global scale, I think that there is globally a need for more vehicles, more transportation capabilities so that we can drive up the cadence and the offers, so that transportation does not become a bottleneck to develop that thriving ecosystem in Low Earth Orbit. How does it help strengthen ESA's ability, either better space security or also being able to transport humans independently and do its own work? How does it help with that? The development of these cargo vehicles is an initial capability and it's really important so that in this new ecosystem we can remain partners and not just customers of services provided by others. But the hope or the dream of many of us, and certainly mine, is that it's just a first step and that once those vehicles are operational and proven, we can then further develop them so that they're capable of transporting crew as well. I always like to remind people because it might not be salient to everyone that people like me who have flown to space as European astronauts—in my case, for example, my first mission was on a Russian Soyuz vehicle and my second mission was on an American Dragon vehicle. We never had and we still don't have in Europe this autonomous capability of flying astronauts. I think this is really important for us. Again, it's not because we don't want to cooperate, but when you are in a partnership you want to be in an equal partnership where you can exchange and share. And so for the overall resilience of space exploration, having another crew transportation capability is really important and I think that in Europe we have to step up to that responsibility. You were the first European female International Space Station Commander; you were also the first female ESA astronaut to do a spacewalk. Women have historically been a fraction of astronauts, however, we're getting to parity. There's been some really good bright spots. What's going right with the pipeline? I would say I became an astronaut 15 years ago and back then in that era, female astronauts were definitely still a minority. But boy, it has changed in the last 15 years. I mean, the last selections, both with NASA and with ESA, are pretty much a 50/50 split. And it's not because we've put quotas or anything like that. It's just because a lot has changed in the pipeline, in those careers from which we recruit astronauts, which are the STEM careers. We're looking for engineers, scientists, medical doctors, mathematicians, or people who come from an aviation background. And so there's a lot that happened in the pipeline in the last 15 years, which is incredibly encouraging. I will say, by the way, that an amazing colleague, new colleague of mine, Sophie Adenot, is going to fly very soon to the International Space Station and I mean, she's going to make us proud. I have one last question for you. What should leaders be prioritizing in 2026? I think in 2026 leaders need to prioritize helping teams find meaning in a world that can be disorienting, right? When you are disoriented, it's not so easy to find your path and that thing that drives your effort and your performance every day. And I think that is the ultimate responsibility and probably the hardest thing for a leader to do this year. Thank you very much, Samantha. And thanks so much to you for listening and for watching.
